“ THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
Tlic Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tlx© Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
• cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
Ihe Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and - best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established A us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised. The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover Js equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the ihost
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
strnightfor—ard ever submitted to the Australian
public.

SATURDAY. JULY 23, 1892.
Prolonging' the Agony.
For many months past the “land banks "of
Australia have been smashing one by one
until now only a very few remain afloat on
the troubled sea of finance. Among those
which are gone there .is a melancholy same-
ness, and the story of one is practically that
of all. With about two exceptions the
twelve or twenty institutions that have
departed had not one shred of common
honesty among them all. They were
organised chiefly in order that the directors
might get money by lying and misrepre
sentation, and spend it in w ild eat mines
and race-horses, and champagne, and mis-
cellaneous females with yellow hail- and bogus
jewellery. They w ere got up, most of them,
that the same directors might buy land at a
low figure and sell it to their “ banks ”at six
times its value. Their business was to take
in the savings of the poor, and to beggar the
widow, and to clean out old men, imbeciles,
and cripples ; and not one, so far as we are
aware, made any serious attempt to lessen
the shock in any way to its miserable
victims. They were nearly all run by shin-
ing lights of the Church—by Christians of
high repute and good standing—and 19
Christians out of every 20 proved to be
unmitigated swindlers, without one human
sentiment in their callous souls and
unfeeling hearts. Among all the de-
ceased offices liardly one ever made a
serious effort to retrieve its position ;
they existed only to get in deposits, and as
the deposits came in they were scrambled for
by everybody from the managing-director
down to the office-boy, and when they ceased
to come in the establishments closed up in
peace. The balance-sheets were bogus from
first to last. The auditors, who, like the
directors, were mostly men of religious
tendencies, attested these balance-sheets
unanimously, and either it was that not one
among them all had intelligence enough to
discover that the office he represented was
utterly insolvent, or else they were all willing
to perjure themselves for a small considera-
tion. There is scarcely one bright spot in
the whole dreary record ; the history "f the
insolvent land-banks of Australia is a story
of bushranging and highway robbery from
beginning to end.
* # *
Also, there is the same dreary monotony
about the liquidators’ reports in almost every
instance. Every bank, when it suspended
payment, told the same tale about temporary
embarrassment, and a large surplus of assets,
and a copious reserve fund, and they all
promised that their depositors would be paid
in full, and that a large balance would
remain over for the shareholders. Then it
leaked out by slow degrees that the assets
wore dead men’s bills, and the unsecured
overdrafts of the directors’ relatives, and
fOl 's signed by persons who couldn’t be
found, and scrip in “gold mines” which
consisted of a hole in a desert and a
dead ass, and occasionally a £lO allotment on
a burnt ridge in the wilderness, w hich pur-
ported to be legally mortgaged for amounts
that varied up to £lOOO or more. There-
fore, the uncalled capital was resorted to,
and the shareholders proved to be partly
dummies and the rest ruined tradesmen and
the like, and the final results in three-fourths
of the institutions are—value of securities,
nil ; value of uncalled capital, nil ; alleged
reserve fund undiscoverable ; debts owing
to the bank, worth about one penny in the
alleged £*, and final dividend to the bank’s
innumerable creditors —a blank.
• # *
A few institutions, however, which would
otherwise have gone with the rest, were saved
by a peculiar legislative enactment passed
by the Munro Government in Victoria, and
foolishly copied by the Dibits Government in
New South Wales. This measure originated
with Premier Munro himself, and Munro
was in the banking business. He was
the creator and leading spirit of an institu-
tion which lias since gone to unutterable
w reck. He w as more or less responsible for
its balance-sheets, which appear to be worse
than dubious. Ho announced that his bank
was prosperous when beyond doubt it was
utterly ruined, and lie misrepresented its
financestothe extentseeminglyof £1,000,000,
and then, last of all, ho departed from the
wrath to come by appointing himself
Victoria’s ambassador in England at a
large salary, and left his beggared share-
holders to face the ruin without him. Being
abankerof this sort he introduced a Bill, which,
in the light of subsequent events, appears to
have been actuated by the same motives as
his balance-sheets, and was just as much
above suspicion as his bank, providing
that any financial institution could refuse
to pay its depositors, and still go on taking
deposits, and could stave oft’ its obligations
for three years, provided a certain number
of shareholders and creditors passed a
resolution to that eflect. And by doing this
it could also postpone an enquiry into the
nature of its securities, and an examination
of the dead men’s overdrafts, and the worth-
less I O U s, and the equally valueless scrip,
and the whole reckless system, whereby its
capital and the depositors’ funds had been
gambled away. It w'as, in short, a scheme
whereby the directors could have three
years more to patch the accounts, or to
bolt if the accounts were past that stage,
and all that time they xvere authorised to
take in and dissipate more money, and
thus make the ruin ten times worse than it
was before. The measure failed in some
degree, inasmuch as several of the most
pretentious “ banks ” came down with a run
all the same, but the scheme has kept alive
a good many moribund vampires which
would be much better dead.
* * *
Those banks which survived by virtue of
-Tames Musko’s device did so ostensibly
because a majority of the shareholders and
the depositors decided to “take advantage ”
of the Act. The said Act assumes that the
shareholders and depositors who voted to
that effect were real individuals, and not
myths got-up for the occasion. In other
words, it assumes that the shareholders’
register is absolutely true and reliable in an
institution where —to judge by a long ex-
perience of land-banks in general—every
other document is a fraud and a lie ; and
that, whereas nine-tenths of the directors
whose conduct has been investigated have
proved utter swindlers, therefore, the direc-
tor whose conduct has not been investigated
is strictly honest, and wouldn’t pack the
meeting with bogus depositors under any
conditions. Both these assumptions are
utterly unwarrantable. When a “bank”
suspends payment under present circum-
stances, the proceedings are a profound com-
mentary on the wisdom of two Australian
legislatures. Two meetings are called—one
of shareholders, and another of depositors
and debenture-holders. The principal direc
tor, or some other member of the board,
generally occupies the chair. The hall is
filled with individuals who may be genuine
shareholders, or who may be hired fur
tlie occasion, and armed with scrip or
deposit-receipts, which were served out
for this especial purpose only. The
chairman, as a rule, is sole judge if
any objection is raised, and he cheerfully
guarantees the bona-tides of his own dummies
—supposing he has any. The directors are
usually loaded down with the proxies of
absent shareholders and creditors, and these
are sometimes all they pretend to be, and
often they are documents signed by
persons who have not the faintest idea of the
purpose for which their signatures are to be
used, and on other occasions, for all the
security which the Act atfords, they may be
absolute forgeries. Every individual who
attempts to demonstrate that the institution
is ruined and utterly rotten is generally
ruled out of order by the chairman, and
finally a resolution is carried whereby the
Board gets three years longer to pursue its
nefarious course without interference or
independent investigation.
• * *
About nine-tenths of the “banks” which
collapsed before this system came into force
have proved, on complete examination, to be
utterly bogus and ruined beyond recovery,
and there is no visible proof that those
which have collapsed since then and have
staved off their creditors under the Act are
any more solvent. The directors of the
banks that are gone have, in at least 50 per
cent, of the cases, turned out to be highway
robbers and keepers of fraudulent accounts ;
the other directors who have successfully
prevented an investigation into their conduct
may be, w ithout exception, men of unstained
honour, but the odds are much against them.
Among the dead institutions w r e cannot at
the present moment recall one which pro-
mised, if it had been allowed to postpone its
liabilities for a century, to pay them in full
at the end of that period by any honest
process, and the chances are that those
which have postponed the evil day will
smash a great deal more heavily at the end
of their three years’ grace than they would
do to-day. The interval only allows the
leading cormorants time to scoop in more
deposits and misappropiate them, to transfer
their shares to men of straw and thus get rid
of future liabilities, or to conceal their assets
and become men of straw themselves, to cook
the accounts judiciously or else have the books
destroyed by some unforeseen catastrophe, or
to gather up their plunder and vanish from
the scene. The Skills Government in
Victoria has already realised that the typical
“land bank” is* utterly incurable, that to
w-ait for it to become solvent is as hopeless
as to wait for the rising of the dead, and that
there is nothing to be gained by prolonging
the agony of slow insolvency. Therefore it
proposes to repeal the &ct of last session,
and let the last of the bogus financial insti-
tutions go with the rest. It is devoutly to
be hoped the Dibits Government will
follow a similar course. The “ bank
which professes to live by borrowing
money at nine per cent., and lending it out
at eight, and which pays ten per cent, divi-
dends by the process, will never become
sound, even though Moses and the prophets
should rise from the dead, and the Govern-
ment which hopes to save it by giving it
time is too hopeful for this world. It isn’t
time which the average laud bank requires ;
it is eternity.
Griffith’s Three-in-One Puzz'e.
Premier Griffith, of Queensland, who drafted
the now-scorned Federation Bill, has built up a
hideous design for a new 7 Constitution for what
are to be “ The United Provinces of Queens-
land” From the title of his Bill, which is to
be called “ The Queensland Constitution of
1892 Act,” to the dead finish, the measure reeks
with rampant Toryism and party artifice. Yet
.Griffith hopes to get the abortion through the
House, since it is believed that otherwise the
Imperial Government will grant absolute
separation to one, if not to two, agitating dis-
tricts, and Bir Samuel has staked his political
reputation on keeping Brisbane and the South
dominant over the whole of Bananaland. Grif-
fith’s Bill proposes three provinces and four
legislatures —one legislature to each province,
and one federal legislature supreme over the
three. But, whilst South Queensland is to
have a double-barrelled legislature of two
Houses, besides being the seat of the Federal
Parliament, Central and North Queensland are
each to have only one House. This experiment
of working different parliamentary systems
side by side may have unsuspected advantages.
The whole machinery of “ The United Provinces
of Queensland,” therefore, will, if Griffith has
his way, consist of—
1. 11 General Assembly ” (or Federal Parliament) made up
of—(«) Senate (elected by Provincial Legislatures);
(b) House of Representatives (elected by provincial
voters).
2. South Queensland Parliament, embracin':—(a) Legisla-
tive Council (nominee as now); (b) Legislative
Assembly (elective as now).
3. Central Queensland Parliament, consisting of an elective
Legislative Assembly- only.
4. North Queensland Parliament, ditto.
The probable constitution of the Senate, under
the genius of party politics, or government by
contradiction, may be at once prophesied.
Each provincial legislature is to send eight
members to the Senate. The party majority in
the provincial legislature is certain, therefore,
to secure eight party scats in the Senate. ■ Sup-
pose three Tory Provincial Governments to lie
in power simultaneously, then 24 Tories will be
fastened on the shoulders of the people as
senators. As their term of office is to be for
six years, or just twice the term of the
House of Representatives, these 24 Tories might
even outlast a change of provincial Ministries,
and be re-appointed at the time of a second
Conservative re-action.
» * «
The House of Representatives is to be popu-
larly elected (with reservations), the number of
members to be in the proportion of 1 to every
10,000 of the population ; which would give 40
members or so to start with, and would cause
the South Queensland influence to preponderate
for many years to come. Each province may
run its own electoral law, so that one province
may adopt one-man-one-vote, while another
may regulate that the standard shall be one-ass-
six-votes-extra. The qualification of a repre-
sentative is to f»e—not merely that he is a recog-
nised provincial elector, but that he has been
for three years resident within the area of the
united provinces; likewise, he must be 21 years
old. and either a natural-born or naturalised
!i '. h subject. Representatives arc to be
salaried, the starting amount nmmi ,
P.m being £lOO But politicians' maythe

PLAIN ENGLISH.
“As True Protestants.”
There is just one thing wanting to complete
the utter degradation of the Jvanakiloriki.'
Cabinet, and apparently that deficiency is about
to be supplied. The Queensland Coalition lias
been supported by the Kanaka and the
“ Patriotic” League and the land-grant railway
syndicates ; and the leper has propped it up,
and the man-stealer has given it his cordial
endorsement; and now the Orange faction
threaten to rouse up the spectre of Dutch
William in aid of Griffith, and to find out
that the Labour-party is engaged in a Popish
conspiracy complicated by crucifixes and thumb-
screws and other horrors. The yellow flaw,
which is the symbol of plague and cholera and
leprosy and of the religious feud which was im-
ported here from Ulster, is to be enlisted, if
possible, in the cause of the parson-begotten
Samuel, provided the followers of the mange-
coloured ensign can only be aroused to the
requisite pitch of frenzy. The following hunk
from a stirring appeal by the “ Grand Master”
of the Orange pandemonium in support of the
Kanaka Government shows that that great
What-is-it, at all events, is solid in the cause :
As true Protestants, and in tlie name of their order,
they should exert every effort they were capable of to kill
that snake in the crass which had come lately into this
colony. It was very <|uiot at tirst, and its movements
were not detected ; but now it was erecting its head, and
could he seen. The serpent he referred to was Socialism,
and it behoved them to see that no man should suffer by
the introduction of that serpent. Every Christian man
throughout the colony should use his utmost endeavours
to make it dig, and die at once. The fact of this bavins;
got hold of our working man was one of the principal
things which had led to the present miserable state of the
colony.
The keynote of this howl is the first three
words: (t As true Protestants.” It is the curse
of all organised fanatics that their fanaticism,
if skilfully manipulated, can be used for all
manner of purposes. There is, of course, no
more reason why the Orangemen, “as true
Protestants,” should object to the White
Labour party, than there is why they, as
bullock-drivers, should object to the bottom of
the Atlantic Ocean, but they only require to be
sufficiently worked up by a wild whirl of words,
and judicious allusions to the capture of
Limerick, and they rciU object unanimously.
Where - two or three hundred cranks are
gathered together for the mutual admiration of
their own diseased little fad, that fad presently
swells up in their imaginations and obscures
the earth, and the entire solar system revolves
round it ; therefore everything that happens
must be in some way connected with it either
favourably or otherwise. Only a very few
years ago some of the football cranks of
New South Wales—being firmly convinced that
the world was run by football —solemnly con-
sidered the rival fiscal policies then before the
country, regarded in their relation to football:
and finally gave a block vote for Freetrade on
the ground that it was somehow more favour-
able to football than was Protection, In the
same way the cricketers and high-jump men of
the same province arc now being appealed to
by the National As-s to support the famous dual


“ vote for Thrift ” in the interests of cricke
mid high jumps. There is no limit to the
results that- may he achieved in this fashion,
j'lic Baptists of Australia, if worked up to a
frenzy by an eloquent agitator, could be induced
—solely as Baptists and in the interests of
adult immersion—to rise in frantic protest
against the dugong-oil industry ; and the anti-
vaccinationists, if sjtilfully manipulated, would
get up in fierce insurrection against schnapper-
tishing. When the infatuated Orangeman
j s invited, as a true Protestant, to prop up
cheap Kanaka-labour, land-grant railways, the
falsification of the electoral-rolls, and all the
other doings of the Griffilwraitii Cabinet, the
chances are that he will do it in the end, pro-,
vicled the appeal is made with sufficient dexte-
rity and persistence. No one knows what a
crank may bring forth, and it is quite within
the bounds of possibility that the Orangemen
will yet rise up unanimously to support Kanaka
slavery, because of the siege of Londonderry
and the victory at Aughrim, and that it will
lake them years to realise that the Kanaka
never was at Aughrim, and was in no way con-
nected with that battle. It will be rough on
William of Orange if he has to vote for
Griffith, but still he had to vote for Parkes
(and Abigail, of the Australian Banking Co.)
not so long ago, and perhaps the dead king is
growing used to such things.
The Doleful Dailies.
Lord Salisbury played a cruel joke on the
Australian daily press. By arranging the
British elections so that all the Tory “dead
birds ” should come off at the outset, he induced
the “loyal” rags of this hemisphere to gloat
prematurely over the inevitable defeat of Glad-
stone's Irish policy, and make miserable exhi-
bitions of themselves when the trouble was all
over. The persistency with which some of
them hung on to their fiction until there were
no figures left to prop it up, and the lie and
liars had to fall with a sickening thud together,
was truly dogged. They left the Home Rule
party out of their alleged calculations as long as
possible, and when the Irish Nationalists came
to be elected they were classified as Parnellites,
and anti-Parnellites, instead of being lumped
together as G lads ton ites, The Orangeism of
Ulster got credit for being a new and mighty
influence antagonistic to Gladstone, when all
the while it was as old and yellow as ever, and
not a bit more mighty than usual. The “steady
Conservatism of the counties ” was taken for
granted in face of the fact that Hodge has been
growing rapidly Radical since he began to
master the theory that his stiff-backed land-
lord is his hereditary enemy. The awful
significance of the reduction in Glad-
stone's majority for Midlothian was enlarged
upon ad nauseam. Also, the petty, prevaricating
dailies shut their eyes to the circumstance that
the Tory party in Edinburgh had accomplished •
a master-stroke of policy by hiring all the local
cabs at one fell swoop. In fact, the heavy
Press of Australia toiled and moiled away at
the idea that Salisbury wasn't done for until
Gladstone got up and thanked the country for
“doing Salisbury bad. ’ Then there was nothing
left for our “ leading ” organs but a groan, and
they groaned like so many town pumps in
distress.

A Rogue and a Vagabond.
The Age, speaking of the alleged robbery of
£4OO from an employe of Skinner, the local
caterer who was taking home his master’s
money :
The detectives have already mentally credited the
authorship of the robbery to two well-known criminals,
who are also suspected of complicity in the descent on
Albert Park railway-statlo.il. Suspicion, however, will
not secure the conviction of this man or his associate on a
definite charge, but it it is ultimately found impossible to
sheet home the robbery to them, an effort will be made to
have (hem dealt with under the Vagrancy Act, a statute
which enables the authorities, when they reasonably sus-
pect a person of an offence which they are hardly able to
prove in detail against him, to have the suspected person
put out of harm’s way for a more or less lengthened
period as a rogue and a vagabond, two notations house-
breakers, of whose connection with recent outrages there
"as no moral doubt, being sent to Pentridge for two
rears, with hard labour, last week by the Carlton Bench.
In other words, it is permitted in this great,
free, and enlightened land, that the two
individuals shall be gaoled all the same,
"Tether they are found guilty or not. The
trouble is over the loss of this man Skinner’s
Money, and if the money can’t be found upon
die suspects, and no one can prove that they
were anywhere about the place where the crime
was committed, and there is no more evidence
igainst them than there is against Skinner
himself, or Premier Siiiels, or Queen Victoria,
- le y "dll be sent to prison for two years or so,
m a charge of “ being suspected.” A cheerful
distance of how this law works is just
rn bounced (in the same Age) from Southern
.11 ' 4 y° un g mau there reported that he
had been violently x’obbed of £27, and the police
straightway went forth and arrested three
3 "agmen who looked poor enough to be run in
"ith impunity. Not one cent, however, could
he found upon them, nor was there the slightest
evidence against them, so the law reluctantly
tain promised by giving the two poorest-looking
!’ nu ‘ s 11 month in gaol on a general charge of

burglaries where the thieves get in through an
unsecured window and loot some lady’s
jewellery the chances are about even that the
jewellery was in pawn unknown to the lady’s
husband long before the burglars arrived ; and
w hen some city office is opened and the safe
rilled “by the use of duplicate keys and
apparently by some one well acquainted with
the premises” it is always worth remem-
bering that the tenant is well acquainted
with the premises. The average robbery
ease is an exceedingly unsatisfactory busi-
ness. There is one man who says he has
been robbed, but beyond his bald assertion
there is frequently no reliable proof whatever.
There is another man who is said to have com-
mitted the robbery that is said to have taken
place. Sometimes there is some property found
upon him, which is asserted, on the unsupported
evidence of the other man, to bo part of the loot
which he asserts that he lost, and in such a case
conviction is almost certain. At other times
there are no material evidences, and then the
law reserves the right to sentence the prisoner
for any term up to two years on a charge of
being suspected of having committed a crime
which is suspected of having occurred. And
of such is the British Constitution and the
liberty of the Australian citizen.
Gladstone's Victory.
Every day the Liberal majority in Great
Britain gets larger, and the ruin of the Tory
Government grows more complete. The famous
Unionist party has faded into a very small
affair, and the Parnellites have practically
vanished, but the followers of Gladstone
and the Irish Nationalists, under McCarthy,
already form a working majority. The Tory
press in England, and the Tory English papers
(such as the S.M. Herald, and Echo)
which are published by mistake in Australia,
now try to extract some frozen consolation from
the fact that in the actual polling the Liberals,
despite their big majority in the House, were not
far ahead. But almost all the faggot votes ”
were cast on the Conservative side, and it is
cold and melancholy comfort to reflect that the
suffrages of men and asses and bricks-and-
mortar and landed property all combined
couldn’t secure even a small majority over what
consisted in a far greater degree of manhood
votes. Probably the return of Gladstone for
Midlothian, despite his reduced majority, was
the greatest triumph of the campaign. * Mid-
lothian is one of the strongholds of the des-
perately religious class in a desperately religious
country, and when Gladstone went to this
pious region with his programme for the dis-
establishment of the Scotch Church he was
tempting Providence in a reckless fashion.
The landlords, the big territorial magnates,
the clergy-—who are so numerous that one
can hardly throw a brick in Midlothian
without braining a solemn, black-coated, black-
hatted preacher, the majority of lawyers—and
every second man in the city of Edinburgh is a
lawyer, and almost every lawyer takes round the
plate on Sunday—were against him, and only
the miners and the democratic working-men
carried him through. The Irish element w'as
with him, of course, but in the east of Scotland
this is hardly worth counting. Midlothian is a
region where the church-bells boom all day long
on Sunday, and where every individual with
the slightest pretence to respectability goes to
service twice and dines on cold victuals in
between lest the Sawbath should be desecrated
by cooking. Also, there is a periodical
“ fast,” and then business is suspended through
the week, and the people go to church for about
four days consecutively. The Presbyterian
Assemblies sit there once or twice a year, and
for a week or a fortnight at a stretch the dreary
preachers make dreary speeches to each other,
and then the daily papers publish gigantic sup-
plements with all these speeches in full, and
print enormous editions, and the people rush to
buy them that they may wade through the
awful mass and treasure it up, and keep it
for reference. Also, the editors issue leading
articles on mysterious points of doctrine,
and whenever some preacher who is a
shade less obscure than the rest “pegs out,”
a biography of him is rushed on the market.
Midlothian suffers, too, from revivals and
incessant street preaching, and it is hard to get
a billet there unless one is solid on the old,
cast-iron Calvinism which damns everybody
who doesn’t go to the kirk, and the majority of
those that do. When Gladstone could carry
this sanctified county in the interests of church
disestablishment, then Conservatism is pining
away more rapidly than even its enemies
imagined.

The address of millionaire Bitrdekin to the
electors of the Hawkesbury (New South Wales)
sparkles with unconscious cynicism. It occupies
a space of three inches, and after reciting that
the candidate has been associated with the
district from boyhood, has, it will be admitted,
effectively represented East Sydney, and has
been Mayor of Sydney, concludes that “you
will perhaps think it unnecessary for me to go
into details.” Of course it is unnecessary.
There is no political detail about Mr. Bricks-
and-mortar Bukdekin. He is the great Bur-
dekin, and that should satisfy any constituency.
His idea of “effectively” representing a con-
stituency, as exemplified by his representation
of East Sydney, is to attend the sittings of the
Legislature say once a week, unless specially
sent for to help the Ministry in a division. The
gallery always knew that things were getting
mixed when, in the small hours of the
morning, Bukdekin suddenly made his appear-
ance on the back Ministerial bench in a
rumpled white waistcoat and a dishevelled
necktie, having obviously been called out of bed
in a hurry. Effective? We should say so.
Who ever heard of an ineffective millionaire ?
The terms are contradictory. One might as
well speak of a calm storm, or a poor retired
policeman, or a weak thunderbolt. The address
finishes by the statement that Mr. Buhdekivs
sole interest is “the advancement of lids my
native countrv. That should lie enough surely
for any constituency which simply reckons up
the lightning speed at which Mr. Burdenin' has
been shoving along lids Ids native country all
the lime that he was “ effectively ” representing
Tarn worth ami East Sydney. Why, look what
he lias accomplished already as a legislator !
Get a microscope and look—hard !
Mu. Justice Stephen (N.S. W.) has managed to
solve pretty conclusively, although, perhaps,
without absolutely recognising his own effi-
ciency, a very complicated question as to who
should possess certain moneys devised by a
married woman, deceased. The lady had
insured her life for £5OO, and made the specu-
lation a success by expiring five years later.
By her will she appointed her husband and two
other gentlemen to be executors. We are not
aware whom she intended to have the money,
but that is a mere detail which Judge Stephen
has deprived of the slightest importance. The
husband had meanwhile become bankrupt, and
his official assignee disputes with the executors
possession of the cash, while the Insurance
Company holds on to it until the rights of the
matter be decided. Mr. Justice Stephen has
now settled the whole thing. He has ruled
that the official assignee shall bring a “friendly ”
action against the Insurance Company, just as
a matter of form, to clear up things. This vir-
tually decides the main contention : who is to
have the money? The lawyers, of course. The
deceased should be glad she died. If she
hadn’t she would probably have been ordered
to pay costs.
Gladstone has won his Midlothian seat by a
majority of 690, and as his majority at last
general election was something like 3000, the
Tory press tries to whoop over the reduction,
and states that the figures are “ a virtual defeat
for the Liberals.” But as Gladstone is in, and
as he gets in equally whether his majority is
5000 votes or whether it consists only of the
casting-vote of the returning officer, and as he
is likely to stay in as long in either case, this
is very dreary consolation. And now the situ-
ation in England is Somewhat anomalous, for
Gladstone— who has had to obtain the consent
of the people ere he could re-enter Parliament
has a majority, in the Commons by the will of
the nation, and Salisbury— who hasn't had to
submit himself as a candidate to the people at
all, simply because, being the son of his
father, he was born into Parliament—•
has a majority in the Lords by the will of
550 fossil landlords, more or less. Also, Salis-
bury’s majority in the Lords might have been
larger than it is but for a loss which fell upon
the peerage years ago, owing to a certain
lamented earl having gone to be with his
fathers. This deceased gentleman was one of
the Earls of Strathmore, and he had the
misfortune to be born an idiot with hair all
over him, and long arms like those of a
monkey. He never talked, but he could
bark and howl admirably, and he generally
went on all - fours, and lived on raw
chops with lots of blood and gristle in
them. He used to snap at his chops with
Ids teeth, after the manner of a dog. Legend
also affirms that this peer had a tail and pos-
sessed one big eye in the middle of his fore-
head. He spent most of his time chained up
somewhere beneath his ancestral halls, and he
had a keeper who used to scrub the animal—we
mean the peer—every morning, and the noble-
man used to bite the keeper when he could get
at him. It has been asserted that this unfor-
tunate gentleman was licensed under the Hog
Act, and at the same time paid taxes as a
human being, so that the State got at him in
both his capacities, but this is also mere legend.
However, the point which most affects Lord
Salisbury at the present crisis is that the
above-mentioned peer is dead. He was a sound
Conservative—at the end of a chain—when he
was alive, and if he were only available now he
might come down to the House of Lords on all-
fours, with his keeper, and give a valuable vote
against Irish autonomy, and the demands of the
Labour Party, and the people generally. Just
now even one vote is a matter of some import-
ance to Salisbury, and he would probably give
something to have that hairy supporter back
again.

A “ leading” Melbourne paper has lately been
bombarded by letters from bank-swindle victims,
offering to produce proof incontrovertible of a
certain exalted person’s rascality. But as each
letter reaches the editor he covers it up and
burns it in a dark corner, and shakes his head
and wants to know nothing, and comes to the
inglorious conclusion that the welfare of Victoria
depends on never making serious accusations
against persons of “ eminence.” The theory
that every man is honest until he’s found out,
isn’t broad enough to defend the supposed
interests of the State in its present predica-
ment, because the finding-out is virtually
accomplished already. I'lie theory that
a man can’t be guilty unless he’s run
in and convicted, is set up to justify the
shameful doctrine that he mustn’t be brought
to account for fear of frightening the British
capitalist. Now, Heaven help the country whose
caretakers are so shortsighted as to think that
it is better to wear dirty linen, which every-
body knows to be dirty, than to wash it in
public. The French and English Canadians
knew better than this, or Mercier and his
accomplices would never have been prosecuted,
and that “ Imperial ” flunkey, the dead Sir
John Macdonald, might have decomposed in
an odour of sanctity.

An Argus leader on the iniquity of Income-tax
was loaded up with the lamest logic that ever
went home to a Tory heart. After a column of
argument intended to prove that a man with
£5OO a year is poorer than a man who lives on
£ 1 a week inasmuch as the former spends his
income in supporting a family, and the latter is
not supposed to have any family at all—the
Argus arrived at this solemn conclusion : “ The
middle classes who would pay the tax would,
we believe, almost unanimously, and certainly
by an overwhelming majority, object to the
impost at this particular moment, and there-
fore, if it be carried, it will bo carried by the
votes of those who do not mean to bear any
share of the burden themselves." What sort of
estimate must the Argus form of the mental
capacity of its readers? No large legislative
Act ever was or ever will be passed by the
influence of a minority, whore pickets a* -
hound to suffer by the transaction. Absentej
landlords don’t rush over to Australia to vote
for an Absentee-tax. Hotel-keepers don’t rise
up as one man and demand a further impost on
rum. And the supporters of the Auers have
yet to wait upon Graham Berry with a request
for increased duties upon all articles which the
well-to-do man regards as necessities and the
poor man never dreams of having in his humble
dwelling.

Melbourne Argus publishes, without comment
or condemnation but merely as an ordinary
news item and a matter requiring no special
notice, that the men engaged on the Buudaberg
sugar plantations “who took an active part in
securing the return of the labour candidate,
have received notice of dismissal.” Also in the.
same issue the same Argus prints a fear-*
some article, blasting a person in the flour-
milling business who. “ sacked ” a non-union em-
ploye. Apparently, therefore, in the view of
the Argus, it is a deadly sin to discharge a
blackleg in Melbourne, but it is rather 'com-
mendable than otherwise to terrorise the
electors of Queensland in the interests of Capital
and Kanaka slavery. The Argus, in fact,
seems to view this species of political corrup-
tion as a matter of course, and considers it a
peaceful, everyday matter that the man who
doesn’t voteas his employer tells him to should be
starved into submission, if the boss can manage
it. It doesn’t even mention that this is boy-
cotting in its vilest aspect, though it denounces
boycotting—when practised by the workers -
in words that make the printing-machine red-
hot ; and though, when the men stop work, it
calls their proccc lings a “ strike,” and demands
that they be put down by the arm of the
law, yet when the employers stop work it says
nothing about strikes. Moreover, being obtuse
and very blank in the intellect, it doesn’t even
know enough to suppress ugly facts which give
its party away. Any capitalistic paper with
enough brains to keep it from falling over its
own big feet would have left that Bundaberg
item out.
The law relating to British naturalization
definitely arrogates to Great Britain a domin-
ancy over British colonies. A foreigner who
takes out naturalization papers in Great Britain
is thereafter a Britisher all over the world. An
alien who naturalizes in a British colony
is only a Britisher within that colony, in the
same way that an Australian “ Honourable ” is
only “Honourable” within his own colony—
and frequently not there. Directly a colonially-
naturalized Britisher leaves a colony in which
he obtained his papers, he becomes an alien
again. (What price Unmack, the Queensland
P.M.G., if he attends an Intercolonial Postal
Conference?) Now, the Queensland black-
birding law regulates that no alien can use a
Queensland vessel for kidnapping—the Britisher
is the only officially-recognised pirate—so that
a captain who has been naturalized in Queens-
land, and not under the Imperial Act, and who
therefore finds himself an alien directly he is
three miles off the Queensland coast, is debarred
from becoming a slaver. There are several
such captains in this predicament, and they
are howling out badly about it.

The awfulness of the Jok Olarke-chw-Chaha
Parker scandal has not yet been duly appre-
ciated. Millionaire Joseph Clarke took oath
and swore that his friend the woman Parker,
or somebody else, forged his signature to a
£5OOO bill, whereupon she was committed to
prison for trial. So friendless was Mrs. P., in
the first instance, that she couldn’t raise a
couple of £lOO bailees. Called upon to prose-
cute the delectable Joseph found that ill-health
compelled him to keep to his bed, and the case of
Mrs. Parker was solemnly adjourned. Then,
two or three days before the adjourned date for
proceeding with this serious charge, a couple
of strangers in the street were hired to go and
bail Mrs. Parker out of prison, and, once out,
the alleged felon was promptly spirited away.
It was a clumsily-managed business. The two
men who found the bail let daylight into their
share of the transaction before they were sud-
denly and mysteriously induced to shut up, yet
no official notice is taken of the obvious fact
that they and oilier people have been
“ squared." Now, at whom docs the finger of
commonsense point as being the motive power
in Mis. Clara Packer's disappearance?

SIC Macquarie-strcet North, Sydney, July 20,1892.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
Noticeable things in “ The Gondoliers” revival at Her
Majesty's were the little hole in Bracy’s near-side blue
stocking, and the way Flora Graupner kicked. Flora
is an engaging little personage whose make-up is a dream
of naturalness, but she ought to moderate her prance or
lengthen her skirts, or something. 1 can’t put down in
plain ink all that flashed through my mind after one
hilarious twirl, but if the lady wants to know very much
I shall be pleased to enlighten her under a double seal.
Miss Young is sartorially doomed as Casilda —such worn-
out styles and worn-out colours!—the real reason why she
is blushing a little too furiously just now perhaps. When
the Duke powdered her pretty 7 little nose in the last act,
it seemed a pity he didn’t extend his operations.
Violet Varley looked very sweet as Tessa. I like the
distracting little jerk her frocks have to loo'ard—it gives
a perky 7, impertinent effect, very desirable in comic opera.
And Ryley was simply too beautiful for words, in the
white kid tights of act tw 7 0. Apropos, the way 7 the
encores went on Saturday was a proof how superior are
legs to wit. Ryley simply gabbled the “King” solo first
encore, but the dance was intelligibly done and a double
call resulted, so it must have been the fandango that
fetched’em. There is a subtle charm about Rj ley when
he bolts to music, that few galleries resist. “Gondoliers”
is beautifully put on, and the limelight man must feel like
a benefactor of the universe when he centres all his
energies on the charming tableau at the first curtain.
The circle was very top-sparklesome at the premiere,
and theatre-parties gabbled right and left. I am pretty
well inured to the ways of such, but I regret it when Fate
puts me in the middle of a mob of women who persist in
say ing “ horrid,” and bang me on the head with their
fans—not in chastisement, wdiich we all deserve—but in
mistake. It is a serious thing to be in the way between a
woman with a heavy-handled fan and the escort she wants
to tap playfully—“ Oh, you wicked man ! ”
But the radiance of the d.e. was spoilt by too many
blackbirds, Mrs. Flood, Lady Innos, Mrs. Burdekin and
her charming daughter ; Mrs. Pilcher (hut she livened
herewith copper-pink): “everybody 7,” in short, was in
•he undertaker's trade-hue. I hope crow-colour isn’t
coming in again. It’s the ally of the woman who isn’t
quite sure of her taste and feels within her a longing to
go forth in pea-green and scarlet, which she has just
sense enough to curb. “ Black is so safe,” she thinks in
meditating “how to look like a real lady,” and that
■ is the reason why it is identical with so many “impossi-
bilities. But what is move vulgar than the fat woman
fitted like a black beetle in shiny black satin glittering
with bugles ?
Talking chiffons, one or two things seem to be the rage
across tlie pond just now. Pea-green is w orn by every-
body—even by the girl with red .hair—and bonnets have
extravagantly long strings, four inches wide, reaching
quite to the hem of the dress. I think that is an im-
provement on the present short ties which hang just the
. length of a he-goat’s beard. Also earrings, the size of a
penny, made of fine gold-wire, seem to have got London
society by the ear, so to speak.
Her Majesty’s was given over to Public-school children
last Saturday afternoon, and an operetta, “The Wreck of
the Argosy,” was performed by the rising Australians to
the full satisfaction of parents and guardians who
crammed the theatre on complimentary cards. But I
must confess 1 don’t see why an operatic training should
he included in the State-school curriculum. It seems an
unwise experiment with young stage-stricken girls—every
girl now wants to be an actress at 14.

Poussard, Laure, and dozens more.
Jf 1 must he candid, the failure of the French Festival!
was the classic music w hich commenced as people changed
plates for the sweetmeats. A piano and parlour-organ-
were placed in the stress of the guzzling, and Poussard
fiddled while digestions churned, as it were. Madame
Kellcrmnnn, Wiegand, Desiouis, and two lady amateurs-
got through some charming music, winding up with the
“Marseillaise,” sung with much lire and fervour by
Desiouis-the whole company joining excitedly at the
“ Aux armes hut I wasn’t pleased with the idea. It-
seemed an undignified sort of tiling to ask good artists to-
perform while the picnic had ils nose in the trough. A
harp and fiddle rasping “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay ” would
have been ever so much more suitable.
The day wound up with dancing to faultless music on
a wax-smooth floor. And, talking about music’, rather a
funny thing happened. When the Consul finished his t
speech on President Carnot, the “Marseillaise should
hare burst forth, but the string band bad its fascinating
eye on Wiegand, perhaps, and started staggeringly off on
the Belgian hymn ! But it might have been “ The W alch

Which all somehow reminds me that I have received a
copy of Billee Barlow’s version of “ Ta-ra-ra-boorn-de-ay, ’
from Messrs. W. 11. Paling & Co. Evidently the pub-
lishers don’t put much faith in Miss B.’s translation, for

tire happy purchaser can take his choice. Or he can
forge right ahead through the eight verses, with chorus,
if he is in a revengeful mood, and there’s an enemy
present.
The Commercial Travellers’ Ball came off at West’s
rooms, last week, and seems to have been a huge succcss i
according to the society reporters. The Mayor and
Mayoress were present; Mrs. Manning wore a black
gown, and the dressing was, I am told, a radiant thing.

In an age of disbelief, when the main fadisto“shee
the wheelsh go wound,” and everybody’s doll is stripped
to its sawdust, it is very restful to go and gaze at Carl
Hertz at the Garrick, and sit with your mouth and eyes
wide open, without the foggiest notion how, or why, or
wherefore, a dear little white rabbit comes out of a man’s
coat-collar at the back of the stalls, with somebody’s
watch lied to his ear. And how 7 three ducks come quack-
ing out of an empty bath is another tranquillizing
mystery. By the way, it’s funny to see how’ Hertz’s
ducks like the stage. On the first night he couldn’t shoo
the dillies away from the floats. Just here it strikes me
that they’re not the first stage-stricken little ducks one
laughs at behind the curtain.
I don’t think there will he very bare-backed circles at
Hertz’s mystifying entertainment. Most curious it is to
note the nice distinctions my sex makes between what is
a collar-bone show and what isn’t. Hertz’s isn’t. At his
debut the only decently - frocked woman was Myra
Kemble, and she hid herself in a prompt box. Her gown
was a \ ery sweet tiling in rags indeed. The remainder of
the sizeable audience appeared to have its wet-weather
boots on—but a row of full-fig Johnnies, strung along the
side chairs like wagtails on a rail, gave a first night-ey
aspect to an otherwise bald and unconvincing reserve-
area.
In giving away clothing some sense of the eternal fitness
of things ought to be observed. It isn’t kind to give
unsuitable garments to people who are looking for work.
Would a mistress be in a hurry to engage a woman who
asked for employment in a soiled velvet visile half-con-
cealing a forest of shreds and patches? No, she wouldn’t
—she’d give the place to a girl who applied with patches
on her elbows.
Charity is a well-known sin-concealer, but the charity
that is at present apparent on the backs of the unem-
ployed seems to me to constitute an evil in itself. It’s an
odd sort of benevolence that gives a tattered man a rakish
hat to look for a job in, and that forces tan boots and a red
scarf on to an unlucky man in torn pantaloons. Such
peculiar kindness isn’t altogether helpful especially
when you meet a draggled female in an umbrella skirt
and no boots carrying a stale infant in tattered petticoats,
but the latest thing in greenaway caps. The very whitest
people look ludicrously like King Billy under such misfit
circumstances.
People may say indignantly that left-off clothes are
left-off clothes and they’ve only those to give, but when
charity is on the carpet surely a little needle-and-thread
juggling should he used and outr6 styles toned down to
wearable pitch, as it were. And another way of meta-
morphosising the unsuitable would be to send the velvets
and brocades to a second-hand-clothes shop and buy com-
moner stuffs with the proceeds. A little home-dressing
would do the rest of the trick.

It strikes me that that was rather a sweeping conclusion
the doctor arrived at in the case of the unfortunate child
—a pupil of the Parramatta Elate school—who recently
broke a blood-vessel in the brain whilst preparing her
Lome lessons. Medicos seems to have chiefly blamed the
lessons, altho’, according to evidence, the child (aged 10)
Lad been eating a quantity of pork and cheese and other
matter.” The lessons certainly were rather heavy (the
little victim was gruesomely engaged upon an essay 7 on
“Bones”), but I am inclined to suspect that had she
been fed on milk and porridge, and a chop once a day,
and vegetables and fruit, instead of on “ pork and cheese
and other matter,” she would have been alive to-day.
I fear the average Australian infant lias a had
time of it from a dietary point of view 7. It sits at the
same board with its elders, and its spoon is in every dish.
It takes the cayenne after its pa, and runs a neck-and-
neck race with its mother at the teapot, and then its little
stomach revolts, and its brain being overworked at the
same time, its funeral is to-morrow. There is many an
infantile victim to coined beef and pickles.

The directors of the Sydney Daily Tele-
graph have evidently given strict injunctions to
their best reporters that no man who is out of
work is to be alluded to as “Mr.” In the report
of the unemployed deputation which waited upon
Acting-Premier Barton this fact comes out con-
spicuously. First, “ Mr. Black ” said something
and “Mr. Barton” responded. Then, after some
more remarks by “Mr. Black” and “Mr.
Barton,” “ Burgess ” laid his views before “Mr.
Barton,” and lie was backed up by “Scott.”
Also “Daniels” made a few observations,
and then “Mr. Black” and “Mr. Barton”
had a little more conversation, and “ Daniels ”
had another say, and after that the pro-
ceedings closed with a few words from “Mr.
Barton.” A meeting was held the same day on a
vacant allotment in George-street, when “ a youth
whose name was given as G. Donnell presided,”
and “Pettrie,” “Burgess,” and other equally
abbreviated persons addressed the crowd. All of
which is very interesting in its way, though as a
starving man can’t eat the “ Mr.” before his name
ho probably doesn’t feel so much hurt as the
Telegraph expected.
The Bulletin having by chance discovered
the obvious erroneousuess of sundry remarks
which appeared in its issue of July 9th, upon
the character of Mr. Maurice Barnett in re-
ference to the Goode will-case, now hastens
to tender Mr. Barnett an apology. The state-
ments were intended to apply to another and
altogether different individual who was mixed
up in the case, and so far as Mr. Barnett is con-
cerned they were, as will appear on a perusal
of the testimony in court, without founda-
tion. Through the inadvertent substitution of
one name for another, however, the record of this
other person was fastened upon Mr. Barnett,
whose own share in the business was, on the evi-
dence', an absolutely innocent one. We regret our
error, and trust that Mr. Barnett’s character, and
the notoriety the case acquired—Mr. Barnett’s
position in the matter having been widely and
fully set forth in the press reports—will prevent
any injury from following upon the error now
acknowledged.

That mysterious art, wherein the quick
ness of the hand plays it low down upon the eve"
has a good exponent in Carl Hertz, who is now
running his unfathomable show at Sydney Garrick
Hertz is a comparatively young man in an urbane
smile and a tail-coat, and his doings are as hard
to find out as the reserve-fund in aland bank after
James Munro lias gone to England. Sometimes
ho plays alone, and sometimes he plays under the
inspection of a “committee” from the audience
and then the things that happen to that committee
are calculated to make an angel weep, and it
presently retires in a stunned condition, and feels
glad that Hertz didn’t undress it on the stage
without its knowledge, and make its clothes turn
up under the Governor’s bed, and jam its hat
down violently on the head of the Shah of
Persia. One of his most remarkable doings is the
way lie chains down a young man on a board, and
allows the audience to put a private mark on the
padlocks and to keep the keys, and hangs up the
board and the young man together—and then in
25 seconds the man disappears and a lady j s
chained on the same board in his place. Also he
makes one of his assistants sit comfortably in the
empty air two or three feet above the floor, which
seems a wonderfully handy idea, for if the police
ever wanted to arrest that man he might step up
eleven yards into the air and stay there. In
between these feats Air, Hertz runs wild, in
a manner of speaking, and devours time and
space, and drags the mangled
corpse of probability all over the
place, and swallows himself, and
spits himself out again, and
walks on nothing, and drags
live poultry out of the clothes
of the audience, and utterly
flabbergasts a big, smiling in-
dividual who comes on the stage
to investigate, and then all of a
sudden begins to swell up visibly
owing to an accmmlition cf
cards inside his waistcoat, and
who finds literature falling unex-
pectedly out of his hair in large
quantities. Also sundry other
performers throw in a light
comedy entertainment, and at
the end things get so confused
that it is difficult to know
what happens, but there is a
vague idea that Hertz fries a
chop with his eye, or else that
the show winds up with a fight
between an unsupported asser-
tion and a bogus oil-painting,
mid that a ghost is making a
noise like dishes falling down a
lift.
** * *
Miss Jennie Lee has
revived the “ Ticket of Leave
Man,” at Sydney Opera House,
and is telling all over again the
story of that young man who
was a particular fool with a
Lancashire accent, and who
therefore suffers much hard
luck and persecution, and is
finally left damp, ragged, and
with a broken head, in a cold
churchyard, his after-fate being
unexplained. The drama was
obviously written to prove that
virtue always comes uppermost in the end,
however much it may be trodden on, and
in this respect it fails ignominiously, inas-
much as the plaintiff loses all his money,
and is put in gaol on a false charge, and then
rises by much hard work and patience to the
dazzling elevation of a messenger in an office, and
after that falls again to utter want, and is finally
floored by a burglar with a “neddy,” and
his character isn’t definitely cleared, and it is
merely implied that he gets back his old job as a
messenger in consideration of his heroism and the
fact that part of his scalp is knocked off. The
company at the Opera House puts as much vim
into this unsatisfactory drama as can reasonably
be expected—and that isn’t committing this paper
to any rash or reckless assertions. Jennie Lee
looks a bright, convincing little personage in boy’s
clothes, and plays the part of Samuel Willoughby
with a cheerful abandon that is charming to
witness, also she sings with a sweetness that is
altogether foreign to the glaring Samuel’s charac-
ter. Nobody else fills the bill too conspicuously,
or does anything calculated to arouse more sus-
picion than usual. Mr. J. R. Greville makes a
big and flabby detective, and somehow that
capable officer looks a good deal as if he was fed
on pork and tarts and large, fat, oily sausages.
M"r. Jewett tackles his North of England accent
manfully, and gets it down at the beginning of the
play, but probably he doesn’t stun it sufficiently,
for it gets away once or twice. Mrs. Bryer and
Mr. Foul ton are distinctly commendable, and the
rest of the show is reasonably effective. Also,
the stage arrangements are all good, excepting
Hawksnaw’s false red whiskers, which look as if
he had borrowed a crimson door-mat and hung it
on his countenance promiscuously.

The performance of “Dr. Bill” on July
30 in aid of the destitute women and children of
Sydney, promises to be a great affair, and The
Bulletin devoutly hopes that it will be several
times greater than present signs would indicate.
The artists include Miss Myra Kemble, Messrs.
Owen Harris, Edwin Lester, Harold Russell, Geo.
Forbes, G. Walton, Miss Maggie Knight and
others. Good old Alex. Deane and Co. supply the
stage as their contribution, and the performance is
announced as being under the “immediate
patronage” of Governor and Lady Jersey, and
under the “patronage apparently not quite so
immediate —of Cardinal Moran (who will sit right
up when he sees “Doctor Bill *') and many others.
All the gate-money goes to the good cause, every-
thing, down to the two brass bands of the Naval
Brigade and the Permanent Artillery, being sup-
plied gratis; and as there is room in the Centen-
nial Hall for several thousand people, and as there
is no earthlv reason why several thousands more
shouldn’t take tickets and stay away for wmit of
space, the takings ought to be immense.
** * *
Other Sydney amusements remain in a
reasonably prosperous condition. Miss Jennie
Lee’s benefit eventuates at the Opera House on
Thursday (July 21) when the Chief Justice and
the Ministry and Dan. O’Connor (who has been
unjustifiably maligned of late) and many others
will be present to give the lady a well-deserved
send-off. Also, Dan. Tracey takes his benefit
every night at the School of Arts, and the Gettys-
burg Cycloroma still tells its tale of the horrors
of war, and the mangled dead shriek for assistance
on the canvas daily, and the two Aquariums and
the Waxworks go on as usual.
** * *
That popular songstress, Mrs. Yanderveer
Green, whose portrait appears in this issue of The
Bulletin, gives a farewell concert at Sydney Cen-
tennial Hall on Saturday (July 23). A good pro-
gramme has been arranged, and the musical world
is expected to roll up in force and drop a tear over
an artiste who will be much missed.
** * *
A first-night audience of unusual size and
quality filled Melbourne Bijou on Saturday. For
nearly two hours “The Times,” a new four-act
comedy by Pinero, gave promise of a golden
future. Then “ The Times ” (which lasted until
past 11p.m.) grew out of joint; the main satire
of the story, becoming exhausted, clutched at
minor satires for support, and the taint of Ibsen
seemed to creep into the proceedings, and when
the end arrived nobody knew whether the author
was holding all his puppets up to ridicule, or try-
ing to draw tears from a crowded house. “The
Times ” is daringly original, absurdly clever,
bitterly humorous, and as heartless as a profes-
sional beauty. It presents several strong sketches
of character, more or less mean and unlovable,
and suggests ideas enough for half-a-dozen farcical
comedies, but there’s a bee in the bonnet of its
purpose, if it has any serious purpose at all. One
ean’d perceive at a glance why this smart play, so
full of good parts, is so unsatisfactory as a whole,
nor will the public trouble to consider this matter
any more than they will wax enthusiastic over
tlie many excellences in a badly arranged picture.
Probably the chief faults in “The Times” are a
want of contrast in effects and a thin, uncertain
conclusion.
# * #
Bompas, M.P., retired draper and pushing
snob, is on the verge of marrying his unaffected
daughter to a long, slim lord when the triumphant
sassiety march of the family is threatened with
arrest. Bompas, junior, a pessimistic oaf, skulks
uito the home of his progenitor with an Irish wife
and mother-in-law selected from the “lower
orders.” He can’t explain why he plunged into
bus matrimony, and the girl doesn’t seem to know
how it happened, and the shocking mother-in-law
merely howls for instructions as to what had
better be done. Bompas appeals to his social
mentor, an elderly cadger of noble lineage named
iVKmty Trimble, and Monty advises the stricken
mtuer to take the pair of female calamities into
his household and palm them off upon the aristo -
cracy as high-toned visitors, whilst he clothes and
educates them up to his own lofty sphere in life. ■
Mrs. Bompas, junior, posing as a virgin “ en- i
gaged ” to her cub of a husband, practises on the
pianny, and Mrs. Bompas, senior, endeavours to
put a polish on the irrepressible mother-in-
law who has thus sold herself and- off-
spring into gilded bondage. The arrange-
ment works all right until a Parnellite M.P.
comes along and discovers the fraud and threatens;
to expose Bompas unless that typical Tory will
agree to change his principles and join the Irish
Party. At this point the brain of the retired
counter-jumper begins to reel and the comedy
commences to totter. Miss Cazalet, a vitriolic
spinster who runs a penny journal, also lights,
upon the family skeleton, and for side reasons
gives Bompas away in her green-covered organ.
Then Bompas and his degraded wife snivel, snivel
seriously, amidst the ruins of their ambition.
Also the worm Monty is trodden upon, not
because lie is a worm, but because his scheme
failed. Finally, the bulk of the company form an
abject semi-circle on the stage, and the slim lord-
ling pops up unexpectedly to announce his inten-
tion of marrying Miss Bompas, in spite of every-
thing, and tire curtain falls upon tire utter collapse
of the Bompas tribe. Whether their gratitude is
intended for a holy emotion or a comic incident
the author alone can tell.
• * * ;
Brough is the leading feature of the show.
From time to time lie does great things in the
arduous character of Bompas, but his performance >
is very unequal. The temptation to clown may
be better resisted when he settles down to a task
which taxes all his powers, and through which
very few actors could struggle more successfully ■
than he did at a first attempt._ Cecil Ward, as
the contemptible son, played his very best, ditto ,
Titheradge in the part of the Hon. Monty
Trimble. Mrs. Brough is several years too
unmatured, and much too angelic for Mrs.
Bompas. Miss Annie Taylor makes a capital
Irish mother-in-law, whilst Boucicault, Miss
Homer, and the young girly members of the cast
get chances to score. Miss Emma Brontou is a
monstrous countess, and Frank Cates, as the
faithful lordiing, has nothing to do beyond look-
ing slim. (Which reminds us that Cranky has
been advertised to play Hamlet for his benefit, at
the Opera House, on Monday next). One gorgeous
scene, representing the salong of Bompas, suffices
for the four acts of “ The Times.”
** * *
“A Run of Luck” was revived at Mel-
bourne Royal on Saturday with vast success. In
the usual way, this horsey spectacle culminates in
a flat race at Goodwood, but for the present occa-
sion Bland Holt has invented a Grand National
Steeplechase, in order that a dramatic Redleap
may lead his field over the last hurdle, and ruin
the chief villain in a canter. The plaudits lavished
upon the winner when it had weighed-in were so
hearty, that “ A Run of Luck ” may extend over
a second week, in which case several other mem-
bers of the company will receive their meed of
praise anon. Meanwhile, the ostler-cut of Charles
Glenney’s pants seems out of harmony with an
awful yearning to carve the name of “ wife ” upon
his mother’s tombstone. Also, Miss Harrie Ireland
“gets up” after the style of Mrs. George Darrell,
and looks far too wax-like for a country wench.
* * *
At the Princess’s they have not obtained
the necessary patronage for a show as large as
“A Midsummer Night’s Dream,” and the last
nights of the Bard are ebbing away. On Saturday
the Barker-Rignold edition of “Faust” will be
set up, and the Melbourne people who have had
so many kinds of “Faust ” lately, should not miss
this opportunity of renewing acquaintance with
the most jocular Devil of modern times.
** * *
Mrs. Beere is farewelling to satisfactory
business at the Opera House, but the lady doesn’t
carry her admirers away, from the fact that Otho
Stuart is a few sizes too small for his important
place in the ensemble. “Adrienne Lecouvreur”
has been the success of the second season, and in
this garish tragedy Mrs. B. will seemingly make
her actual last appearances to-night and to-
morrow.

Things look sickly at the Alexandra, but
the halls and Waxworks hold their own. Fitz-
geralds’ Circus was a fashionable resort at racing
time and should flourish this week, for the tent is
about to quit Melbourne after a long and prosper-
ous season.

The “ tangi ” held at the death of
Jeremiah, a Taranaki Maori chief, cost his rela-
tives .£5OO. Yet the most reckless old do’ man
would not have “sprung” half-a-crown on
Jeremiah’s outfit in the flesh.
An amorous old Assyrian lady has been
begging round the N.S.W. back country. _ At
Porcupine Gap, recently, when the police examined
her as a possible vagrant, she had £4O on her. At
Parkes, later on, she panned out—hard cash, £l6;
several receipts for registered letters ; receipt for
a horse; receipts for trinkets, etc. A case-
hardened beak sent her up for a month as a pro-
fessional beggar, whereupon the dark-skinned old
girl fell on the neck of Constable Duffy, and kissed
him violently just beneath the moustache; then
she laid loving hands on Editor Bovvditch, who
was at the Press table, and kissed him on both
ears ; next, she tackled Senior-constable "Radford,
and reached his nose with her lips incarnadine ;
next, she embraced two court idlers ; and, finally,
mounted the bench, grabbed Magistrate Harwood
vigorously by the back of the neck and smothered
him with osculations—long, loud-sounding, and
savouring of potential suction.
The Wild-Cat Column.
The Mysore gold-mine (owned in London),
India, is one of the richest in the world. It is worked by
■coolies and pays more than half its capital yearly in
■dividends, besides heavy official fees and salaries. The
plan issued with the last report clearly shows that the
profits arc obtained from a 250-feet run of gold, and that
immense sums are spent in prospecting other parts of the
reef. The lode averages 3 feet and about 2ozs. per ton,
and the output is enormous.
* * •
Concentration of mineral is causing a quartz-
reefing revival, at Maldon (Vic,), where, owing to the
presence of a large percentage of arsenic and oi her silver-
sickening minerals, carrying most of the go id, 'great
•obstacles have been encountered. In one big' mine they
have attached lo a 20-head battery Halley’s percussion-
table ; then 40 feet of blanket-tables. After that
the tailings are further treated in Duncan’s concentrators
and finally pass over ten Triumph vanners into slime
settling-pits. No quicksilver is used in the boxes. The
result of this elaborate concentration is that the-ore
body, which is of great size, returns upwards of lo?. per
ton and is worked at a good profit.

and then proceeds to enumerate good mines commencing
with the Ilivertree Silver Mine and the Rise and Shine
43. M., both of which are in N.S.W.

A certain and sudden death. Ask any M.P. to
accept a seat with fees and free shares on the directorate
■of a new bank or building society.

The announcement that the Great Eastern Lode
■Co. had floated in London was Hailed with delight in one
■quarter. The Sunday mining “tipster” who rushed a
number of people into Bonanza Consols at 10s. and 125,,
■clutched at this remaining hope of reviving the boom because
the Great Eastern was near the Consols which would now
“ come again.” They have come down to a penny, and
are dear at that for several reasons. Even the floating of
the Great Eastern is poor consolation, in view of the
Financial World's caustic comments on the financial
status of the Lord at the head of the concern.
* * *
London, so we are told, is sick of Australian,
particularly N.S.W., ventures. Little wonder. For the
disastrous experience of past investment London deserves
most of the blame, as the greed of brokers, financiers and
promoters generally lias over-loaded every honest spec, to
collapsing-point.
* » *
The last washing-up of the Golden Terrace
Sluicing Co., Shoalhaven river, N.S.W., returned, it is
said, a shilling to the yard—a dividend-paying result.
The preparatory works of the Jessop and Spa Co. are
approaching completion, and as they contemplate sluicing
3000 to 5000 yards per day, even a shilling a yard will
mean a big thing. We foresee the time when hundreds
of men will find work on the Shoalhaven River banks.
* * *
Somebody in London has secured Laboucbere’s
influence in favour of the Charter Towers mines. Truth
is persistent in its advocacy of investment there, and the
result will depend upon how far judgment and discretion
run together. But even “ Wild Cats” of the Towers have
something to recommend them. One floated in London,
some years ago, paid numerous dividends to its share-
holders by striking a rich reef, the existence of which the
promoters never dreamt of.
# # *
How much gold really came out of the late
J. B. Watson’s Kentish claim on Bendigo, Heaven only
knows, for the owner (now there, we hope) never pub-
lished its returns in its richest days, but on the authority
of miners who w’orked there, the mine must have yielded
some hundreds of thousands of ounces. After a long run
of poor stone, the property has been sold for £3500.
« * *
Brown’s Creek (N.S.W.) has again been can-
celled for the non-fulfilment of labour covenants, and
re-pegged. It is a big concern in the hands of
big people with big money and big plant; it is
capable, above all, of a big output. It has a
battery of thirty head with engines, boilers, &c., all
in a state of dilapidation and decay. The ore is of a low
grade, containing the finest gold of low value. It might
be made a paying mine in the hands of a capable econo-
mical manager with £20,000 for development and entirely
new plant.
* # *
The bottom seems to have come out of that
much advertised concern, the Mount Wills Tin Mine,
Gippsland. The first reports declared that it meant a
pile, but the shares have gradually declined and are now
offered at 9d. (£1 paid up).

* * *
True story of the art and mystery of banking as
practised up South: There is a bank of the kind that
hungers after fixed deposits at ever so many per cent.,
and does all kinds of things on commission. One of the
shareholders is an importer. This individual had an order
for a lot of particularly expensive cigars for a club, and
when he imported them the bank kindly put them
through the Customs for him and undertook their
delivery. Somehow they never were delivered—either
the bank smoked them or else it sold them, or perhaps it
smoked some and sold the rest. Anyhow, the club
presently complained that it hadn’t got its cigars, and the
bank offered it the very few that were left—which offer
was promptly declined. Then the bank-manager gave the
importer his own bill at three months to make up the
shortage, and the customer endorsed the bill, and de-
posited it in the bank —the same old bank—for collection.
When it fell due the manager didn’t pay up but gave a
fresh bill in place of the old one. Then three months
more passed, and the manager sent along an intimation
that his own bill had been presented to himself and dis-
honoured, and requested that the tobacconist would
retire the valueless paper forthwith. Therefore the
customer has lost his cigars, and lost his money, and has
to retire the manager’s bill because he rashly endorsed
the document; and the bank still takes in fixed deposits
for three years at the highest current rates as heretofore.

The British Bank collapsed in Melbourne last
August. Its properties, valued in the hooks at £360;0C0
over and above mortgages, have realised to dale £2549,
and not much more is hoped for. The liquidators found
that most of them were hypothecated to their full v alue,
and in most cases they had simply to hand them over to the
mortgagee, and let him get his money out of them any-
how he could, the equity of redemption being utterly
valueless. Then they proceeded to realise the uncalled
capital of £349,000, and by haul and merciless grinding ct
the shareholders they have collected £5OOO in cash anti
£BOOO in pro-notes. They have little hope of gelling any
more, and they don’t expect the pro-notes to he paid,
it isn’t worth while liquidating the ordinary “ hank "
nowadays. When the institution breaks up i! would ho
cheaper to lynch the hoard, sell their corpses to the
Chinese gardeners, throw the securities into the ash-
barrel, and say nothing more about them.

The Melbourne land-boom has been likened to a
piece of putrid meat which resolved itself into a wriggling
mass of maggots. Then the stronger maggots ate the
weaker maggots, and the foul smell of these bloated
survivors will taint the land until the heel of the law
crushes them out of existence.
With the exception of Bell, Davis, and Malcolm,
the entire lot of “financiers’' now cooling or about
to cool in Darlinghurst (juries, nowadays, are in a
lynching humour with regard to all such individuals)
have snent every cent of the proceeds of their robberies.
Even the fellow who potted £17,000 out of one concern
alone had recently to part with his favourite diamond.
The “bonking” boom was simultaneous with the silver
and land rise, and the money squeezed from the “ hanks”
was thus gambled with. The examination of Richardson's
books show that about 75 per cent, of bis £BO,OOO has
been dribbled away by himself and others in land-gambles.
Addy spent his £lO,OOO in mining, and the Victorian
deposit-sharks are also miserably poor.

It was in the ante-cable days. The company
was an English one, and an ugly rumour got abroad that
the mine (a copper one, in S.A.) was the rankest kind of
duffer, and shares on London ’Change went down flop.
The directors met, considered the matter, and instructed
one of their number to hasten to S.A., inspect the mine,
and rush ’(hue again. This honest agent packed his
portmanteau, bought all the shares he could buy (in the
mine he was going to see) without rousing suspicion, and
departed by mail steamer. In due time he relumed, an 1
the directors met to hear his report. “ Well, Mr. ,”
said the chairman, “ You have seen our property. Is there
any ground for the existing bad reports about it ? WeP,
gentlemen, as ynn know, I am not a mining man and
cannot give a technical report, but I have just come from
your mine and I will give you £65,000 for her.” Offer
declined. “Gentlemen, I will go further. I will give
you £80,000.” Declined again, after a little longer dis-
cussion. “Well, gentlemen, I will make my last offer.”
He drew out his cheque-book, and spoke more impres-
sively. “I will give you a cheque for £50,000 now, and
£50,000 more on completion.” “Would Mr. retire
for a few moments?” He did so, and was presently
called in and his offer declined again. “ Well then,
gentlemen, I trust we shall hear no more about our mine
being valueless.” Next day, up went shares. Visiting
director netted £30,000. This was in June. In September
the mine was hung up.
