It is interesting to read the guileless statement
of the S.M. Herald that the representatives of the Broken
Hill companies met together and resolved unanimously,
“ after careful and repeated consultation,” that though
they were pledged to arbitrate over all future disputes,
and though they were also pledged not to slope ore by
contract, yet they were at perfect liberty to slope by con-
tract, and the arbitration clause didn’t apply. And just
here one sees a vision of Deeming meeting together and
resolving unanimously that though he had committed
murder yet the hanging laws didn’t apply to his case at
all; and James Munro passing a resolution whereby he
was acquitted of all responsibility over the Real Estate
Bank; and Abigail deciding—after careful and repeated
consultation with himself, and after laying the matter
before himself as an entirely disinterested party, and
having first appointed himself referee in case of any dis-
pute—that his company is perfectly sound, and is a
flourishing concern to this day.

SYDNEY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
South Australia is about to re-enter the
federal Council.
Maoriland will be the first Australasian
country to try Female Suffrage.
One of the N.S.W. Labour members has
just been elected to Tattersall’s Club. This doesn’t
look like Labour.
Sir John Downer (S.A.) says he has
learned to look upon political somersaults not as
demoralisations, but as developments.
The bitterest assailants of the Labour-
party in the Queensland Assembly are the
members for whom the grave of political obscurity
is yawning.
“ It is a very wet night when the Women’s
Suffrage League cannot hold a meeting,” says
Sydney D.T. In other words, it’s a cold day when
women don’t want to talk.
A strong Farmers' Political Alliance has
been organised on the Downs (Q.). It promises to
be an anti-Griffilwraithian bulwark, an ally of
Labour, and an element to be reckoned with in
the future.
Mr. Henry Austin, wool-buyer: “There
are far too many sheep in N.S.W'.; the country is
‘sheep-sick.’ There are too many men in the
cities, and Sydney is men-sick. Wanted, more
agriculture.”
Deeming lost his life through not having
been a member of the Victorian Legislative
Assembly. After clearing out to Western
Australia no M.L.A. could fairly get accused of
anything behind his back.
Money-lender Cohen, striking a declama-
tory attitude in S.A. Assembly : —“ Ido not sea
why there should not be the same standard of
morality in Parliament as in ” “Pawn-
broking,” said a profane Gentile.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.) says that every
Australian must feel proud of being an English-
man. Which is just as logical as if that stunted
groveller had said that every horse must feel
happy to think that he is a cow.
Henniker Heaton, in the Commons :
Prom his knowledge of Australia he pledged his word
that no outrages u’ould occur (in the South Seas) in
future, and that the most kindly feeling would exist
between the native labourers and their employers ! ! !
We have pointed out the regular swing of
the pendulum over the English elections, six
years Liberal and six years Conservative : Glad-
stone, 1868-74 ; Disraeli, 1874-80, Gladstone, 80-6 ;
Salisbury, 86-92 ; Gladstone (or somebody), 92—.
Whenever a Labour member gets up to
speak in the Queensland Assembly the Griffil-
wraith bullies hound him ddwn. The drunken
maunderings of a member of their party (which
never reach print, by the way) can be heard
unchecked night after night in the chamber.
The real Anarchists are the associated
capitalists. The Unions, if destroyed, will be
succeeded by secret societies and the deeds of
darkness which have ever been associated there-
with. This is not a threat —it is but a warning
from the pages of history, ancient and modern.
The awful all-round rottenness of the Vic.
Assembly is plainly indicated by the fact that a
cry for the recall of James Munro came first from
the Council, and has, up to time of writing, been
ignored by the House whose late Premier and
Speaker are objects of honest execration at public
meetings,
Mr. Crick promises to inaugurate a debate
in N.S.W. Assembly concerning the torture, by
Hogging, of prisoners upon whom long sentences
are passed. The legislator who would superadd
floggings to seven or ten years’ penal servitude
(including solitary confinement) is simply a brute-
beast.
O’Shanassy opposed the prayers in the
Victorian Legislative Council, and used to make a
point of talking loudly outside the Bar while the
President read the “ Onr Father.” Only by a
narrow minority, in 1856, did the Assembly decide
not to have prayer. Fancy Speaker Bent leading
off!
Of the S.A. Ministry, Premier Holder and
Chief Secretary Cockburn are howling teetotallers
and fervid local-optionists. Their colleagues,
Stock, Gillen, and Handyside, were publicans’
nominees, and Gordon likes good whisky. Yet
the temperance party hope for great things from
the Holder Ministry.
S. M. Herald has thrown overboard
George Reid, the elected head of N.S.W. Foreign-
trade party. During the reign of Parkes, the
N.S.W. Freetraders contemptuously shrieked to
the Protectionists, “ Bah ! where is your leader ? ”
The N.S.W. Foreign-traders now find that they
have no leader, save the man whom they lately
denounced in chorus as “Traitor ! ”
What is the so-called “dual-vote for
Thrift?” Simply the worship of success? An
enormous number of the rich have not personally
made but only inherited their money. Therefore
the “vote for Thrift” is, in thousands of cases,
only a recognition of the odious hereditary-legis-
lator system, the full recognition of which no
Australian public man dare openly propose.
The history of the Cairns (Q.) railway
contracts would add an interesting chapter to the
“Book of Jobs.” Besides the amount of his
original tender, one of the contractors has already
recovered £lOl,OOO from the Government, which
planked down the amount with scarcely a whim-
per. He now claims another quarter-of-a-million,
and will probably get a big slice of it, too.
A “ Patriotic League ” J.P. has just been
fined up North for roll - stuffing. This good
patriot sat on the Bench in the Revision Court
and helped to pass his own work as correct, so the
disaster which befelhim was very unexpected. It
is only in one case out of a hundred that a man
comes to grief when he has the privilege of acting
as judge in his own case, and nothing but awful
clumsiness could have brought the mishap about.
The reiterated declaration that the sale of
the N.S.W. railways would pay the whole of the
province’s State indebtedness, is disquieting, and
is construed by many people as the mouthing of
the syndicate-mongers who will take advantage of
the coming intensification of the money difficulty
■should the voice of Labour be diminished in the
Assembly. This would mean an immediate 25
per cent, redaction in wages to a corresponding
increase of the hours of every labourer.
ASKING TOO MUCH.
INDIGNANT LANDLORD : “Here! you didn’t pay [?] that beer."
CUSTOMER : “ You've paid for it haven’t you?”
INDIGNANT LANDLORD: “Certainly I have.”
CUSTOMER : “Well, it doesn’t want to be paid for twice.”
Samuel Rowe, a Bundaberg (Q.) J.P., who
outraged the feelings of Property by helping to
secure the return of Labour member Hall a few
weeks ago, has been reipoved from the commission
of the peace by the Griffilwraith Cabinet. About
the only thing that is left for the Kanakilgriff to
do now is to suspend the Habeas Corpus Act, and
start arresting all his prominent opponents pro-
miscuously. Rowe used to take his table out in
the main street, on Saturday nights, and endorse
the workers’ electoral form claims.
The monopolists have started a wage-wa 1 ’
in Australia. They are determined to lower the
standard of living. What is really wanted is a
crusade against high rents. When a wage-earner
pays away a third or fourth of his earnings, say
from 10s. to 255. weekly, in house-rent, he is only
working for the landlord. If the wage-earners
would only arise in their strength and force upon
the various Governments such schemes as are
set forth in the “ Labour Town ’ project now
or lately before the N.S.W. Cabinet, the bricks
and mortar monopolists, with thousands of empty
tenements upon their hands, would soon be
brought to reason.
Two thousand Maoris (accompanied hy
three Maori brass bands, totalling 70 players)
lately assembled at Hawke’s Bay and elected by
ballot a Parliament of 00 members, with Speaker,
chairman of committees, cabinet, chaplain, &c.
Then they proceeded to discuss the land-question,
tempered, with football. The Maori Parliameu
will not sit at night, nor admit the public, no
run Hansard, The refreshment-room is grogless,
and drunkenness is to be punished with three
days and a fine. By the way, the white govern-
ment’s proposal to admit alcohol into the Maori
King country raised such a storm throughout the
district that it had to be withdrawn, and thus the
land-sharks who wished to decimate the dark men
are foiled.
C. E. Jones—“ Mint Sauce”— lias gone
back to the shears, the goose, and the cutting-
board, in Melbourne, after failing as politician
and editor.
Wired that Cyrus Field, the first man to
lay the submarine cable, is dead. They may fairly
carve upon his tombstone: “ Here lies the father
of lies.”
Max O’Rell is now, or has just been, at
Broken Hill, seeing “The Australian On Strike,”
which will be the heading of a chapter in his
forthcoming book.
B. D. Morehead, M.L.A. (Q.), holds that
“no person who cannot read and write should
have a vote.” On the same principle, no person
unable to read and write should be gaoled for any
offence whatever. If a man is allowed no voice <in
the making of the laws that affect his daily life.
he can be guilty of no offence against those laws,
from his own point of view. Morehead also is
the one legislator of Bananaland who has consist-
ently and persistently decried the uselessness of
free education, and scoffed at the enlightenment
of the masses. He would thus compel the workers
to remain unlettered and then disfranchise them
for being unlettered. Which is just as brutal and
unjust as if the State compelled Morehead to be a
squatter’s jobber, and then hanged him for being
a squatter’s jobber.
Sydney Burdekin, whose only real political
qualifications are his monstrous money-bags, and
the manner in which they are worshipped by the
greasy gang who sponge upon him, has made a
spectacle of himself, or the Foreign-trade party
has made a spectacle of him, in the rush
after the Hawkesbury (N. S. W.) electorate. Before
Bowman was dead, Burdekin’s supporters were
making arrangements for the approaching elec-
tion, and they started canvassing the district
before the grave was dug, and while yet the
doctors were not absolutely certain that deceased’s
pulse had struck work. The Bulletin recom-
mends the Hawkesbury electors, in default of a
good protectionist candidate, to vote for Burdekin’s
Foreign-trade opponent, W. E. Morgan. We
have no proof before ns that Morgan can think,
but he has a lovely tenor voice, which is as good a
legislative qualification as a hundred rows of
houses. But, anyhow, it apparently doesn’t much
matter who represents the Hawkesbury. There
are, in N.S. Wales, some sets of electors who don’t
get huffed even when their member stays drunl-
for twelve years at a stretch before he dies.
The arrest of Francis Abigail, N.S.W
ex-Minister of Mines, on a general charge of
taking part in the publication of falsified accounts
in connection with the Australian Banking Co
will be a serious shock to a lot of people. Abigail
is chiefly know'n as a man of cast-iron integrity
and the fearful rackets he made in N.S.W. Par-
liament some years ago about the alleged barbed-
wire and Standard-paint swindles, and the dump-
car business, to say nothing of his determined
attempts to gaol the members of a big fim,
accused of conspiring to rob the N.S.W. Railway
department by means of fraudulent waybills, haVe
not yet been forgotten. Abigail was always a
man of bloated purity and unnaturally enlarged
conscience, and lie loathed the very appearance of
evil. He was Minister of Mines in the Parkes
Cabinet, and was once accused by some rascal or
other of using his position for Ids own profit or

The Balmain (Sydney) Ferry Co. have the
monopoly of the tw'o new Government wharves at
Erskine-street for <£3oo a year. The N.S.S. Ferry
Co. have absolute command of every useful land-
ing-place at North Sydney, and term after term
they get from Government the renewal without
competition of the lease for Circular Quay jetty.
Consequently, a man with a large family has
annually to pay from £l2 to £lB by way of tolls
for the privilege of living across the “silver
streak.”

Mr. A. Benue 11, Miner, Top Bingara.
N.S.W., writes as follows :
Dear Sir, —It is with the greatest pleasure that
I bear testimony to the wonderful effects of
Clements Tonic. I have been a great sufferer
from Liver Complaint for the past 18 months,
scarcely able to work, the least exertion causing
great fatigue. I did not know what to do with
myself, I felt so miserable; I had no appetite, a
child could have eaten more than I did. One day
a friend gave me one of Clements Medical Guides,
and there I read how Members of Parliament and
other influential people had been thoroughly cured
by Clements Tonic, and I reasoned, “If Clements
Tonic cured them, it will cure me.” I made up
my mind to give Clements Tonic and Fletcher’s
Pills a fair and honest trial. I knew it was my
last resource, and remember I am only giving yon
a true and faithful statement of my case, such as
an honest man should give. My eyesight got so
bad that on four consecutive nights I missed four
of the largest kangaroos that run in the mountains.
After I had commenced taking Clements Tonic I
took down the rifle to clean it, and, after doing so,
I looked along the barrel and could see the sight,
to [my great surprise, quite plainly. I had only
taken three doses, and my sight was wonderfully
clear and distinct. This speaks volumes, for the
effect was simply wonderful in so short a time ; that
night I killed a walleroo at least 150 yards away.
1 cannot speak too highly of the wonderful effect
of your remedies. I had all the symptoms which
accompany liver complaint—headache, backache,
indigestion, pains in the chest, ringing noises in
the ears, specks before the sight, partial blindness,
nasty bitter taste, weakness, pains in the sides,
high colored urine, pains in urination, disinclina-
tion to work, poor appetite, drowsiness and sleepi-
ness ; at night I would jump out of my sleep as if
a sharp instrument was being thrust through me.
I feared I had all the diseases under the sun, so
miserable was I, but I am now quite well, thanks
to Clements Tonic and Fletcher’s Pills. The
pains in the side and chest were cured before I had
taken half a small bottle, and as I continued the
remedy all the other symptoms disappeared. At
one time I was so weak I could only cradle one
hopper-full of dirt at one time, so severe were the
pains in my back and sides. Sometimes a terrible
feeling would come over me, as if an evil spirit
were approaching, which would be followed by
extreme giddiness and prostration. Lots of other
people in this locality have been similarly cured
by these remedies, and I earnestly entreat all
people afflicted as I was to follow my example at
once, for “a stitch in time saves nine,” I have
lived here on and off for twenty years, and have
been settled on the Bingara goldfield continually
for the last six, and am well known. I never intend
to be without Clements Tonic and Fletcher’s
Pills “as long as there is a shot in the locker,”
and I now never fear a relapse, because I take an
occasional dose, and it keeps me in perfect health.
You are at liberty to make what use you like of
this, for I consider it everybody’s duty to give an
account of their case where they have derived so
much benefit from a remedy as I have from
Clements Tonic.—Yours very truly,
A. RENDELL.
Bingara Goldfields, N.S.W,

A certain Australian politician’s house is
now watched, day and night, by four policemen.
He can’t sleep if they aren’t there; the horror of
approaching death is upon him, and the crazy
idea that some enemy is “ laying ” for his life eats
into his vitals deeper every day. i'he time has
come when he is scared by his own footfall, and
his own shadow; he is like Cromwell, wearing
armour under his clothes, and constantly changing
his bedroom to dodge his non-existent pursuer, or
Murad, the lunatic, haunted by the shade of ms
uncle Abdul-Aziz, or Constantine pursued by the
wraith of his murdered son, or Richard 111. with
spectres climbing on his chest, and sitting on his
diseased brain. Nobody shows the slightest sign
of wanting to murder him, but his own frantic
egotism makes him believe that all the Labour
party is after him with concealed dynamite. And
meanwhile his chief political pal sleeps soundly,
and devours his huge breakfast placidly, and
presents his unruffled corporation to the foe in
absolute contentment. The fat man who can
sleep well at night is proof against a sea of
troubles.
Ex-Premier Sir Harry Atkinson’s death
was a meet end to his career. The Maoriland
Legislative Council had just opened its first
sitting, and he was in his place as Speaker. Sir
Bat. Buckley referred feelingly to the deaths of
three members during the recess—Sir Frederick
Whitaker, John Martin, and James Fulton.
Oliver followed suit, and said no one could better
speak of them than Sir Harry, but the latter was
mute, beyond putting the motion for the adjourn-
ment out of sympathy with the relatives of the
dead. Then he walked to his room, Oliver going
with him. Captain Morris complimented him on
his healthful appearance, as did Oliver. “I may
look well, but I have my marching orders,” was
the veteran’s reply. Both chaffed him, Oliver
saying he would live long yet. Atkinson shook
Us head: “No, no; I will die soon—in fact’
(and he put his hand to his chest), “ I do not feel
well now,” and with that he arose, took a few
steps towards his tiny bedroom off the Speaker s
room, and, turning suddenly, said quietly: “Send
someone to me ;I am bad.” In a few moments a
doctor came, but the veteran was lying dead on
his little trestle-bed, still in his official robes,
having whispered to his messenger: “Take my
love to the folk at home,”

“ The jealousy of women is at bottom a
different sentiment from that of men, springing
from different causes. It is interesting to note
this in view of the efforts of the faddists of the
press and the platform to have men judged by the
same standards of morality as women, especially
with reference to breaches of the marriage vow.
Jealousy I believe to be inspired on the part of
men by their innate desire to be sine of the
paternity of the children bearing their name,
while the woman’s sentiment is prompted by the
necessity of defending her position in the house-
hold, and obtaining the protection essential to her
in child-bearing. It will be found on reflection
that this idea works out in accordance with the
ordinary experience of life. A wife’s jealousy is
usually relieved by the assurance that her rival is
inferior to herself in attractiveness, and has no
chance of supplanting her in her husband’s affec-
tions, whereas the condonation of the wife’s
offence on such grounds is entirely foreign to a
husband’s feelings. That either men or women
are conscious of such motives Ido not say. lam
speaking of the origin and the purpose served by
a passion which on both sides is one of the
strongest in human nature.
A. NESBITT.
Fascination.
WHY SO MANY LADIES WHO SHOULD BE ATTRACTIVE
FAIL TO PLEASE—A BRILLIANT WOMAN-
REVEALS THE SECRET.
“ Dress is everything ! "
“Nonsense ! No beautiful woman 6VeT Was or
ever will be improved by a dress, no matter how
artistic it may be made.”
The speakers were two lady reporters for society
papers. They had attended balls, receptions, and
parties for years. They had studied the effects
of costumes, the lines of beauty, and had faith-
fully written upon them in the papers they
represented. They were masters in their art, and
yet they differed.
“You cannot prove your assertion by any lady
of high standing and authority.”
“1 can,” replied her companion, “within two
days I will show you an interview with the
ing woman on dress of America.” And the two
friends parted.
Last Wednesday they met at the sahie place,
when, producing a neatly written manuscript, the
lady read the following remarkable interview :
“ Mrs. Annie Jennes Miller is the acknowledged
authority in America upon the subject of dress.
An attractive woman herself, she knows how to
render other women attractive in the highest
degree. I sought an interview, it was granted,
and 1 give you the results
“ ‘Do you think, Mrs. Miller, that women are
'made more attractive by their dress?’
“‘That depends upon what you mean by
attractive.’
“‘Pleasing, fascinating if you choose—more
charming to men and other women.’
“‘A simply beautiful woman is seldom at-
tractive; a stupid one never. It is the soul, the
life, the brilliancy, which render women attractive.’
‘“I’ll admit that, but what makes women
brilliant ? ’
“‘Vivacity, brightness, and good health. Did
you ever s?e a sickly woman able to entertain a
dinner party of bright people? On the other
hand, did you ever know a cultivated and refined
woman, overflowing with animal life and spirits,
who was not fascinating?’
“ ‘ So you believe perfect health is the secret of
fascination, do you ? ’
“ ‘ Most certainly. Artistic dressing is proper,
tine curves attractive, but life sueli as conies from
healthy, throbbing blood is alone fascinating. It
is a great mistake, however, to think that health
is preserved by dress alone. Women must have
proper food, freedom from care, and a good friend
in need.’
“ ‘ What do you mean by that V
“ ‘ Some assistance, physically. All women feel
depressed at times, and all pleasure seems gone
out of life. On all such occasions, and, indeed,
whenever blue or worn out, she needs help. 1
know, because I have been in that condition my-
sel f. ’
“ ‘ And what do you do when in that condition ?’
“‘One tiling, and one thing only. I am
assisted by the best friend that any woman ever
had. It is Warner s Safe Cure. I mean it, and I
have good reason to speak as I do. You think I
am a perfectly healthy woman ; so I am, but I
take several buttles of this great cure every season,
just as I take additional care in the selection of
tonic-giving food. As you know there are certain
times when every woman needs assistance. At
such times, and before such times, there is, so far
as I know, but one thing that can help, and that
is the great cure I have named.’ ”
“ I bade Mrs, Miller a reluctant good-bye. for I
felt that I had met a woman who knew women—
what their trials and troubles were, and what
they required.”-

It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a-
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. W(j
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,
SO anil 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, I*
just the place ; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before talcing the tram for home.*

# # #
There was a remarkable scarcity of women
at the V. A.T.C. “Unemployed”meeting. Charity
didn’t run to a grand-stand ticket for Madame
in many instances. In fact, as the result proved,
the receipts might have been increased if the Club,
instead of withholding the usual complimentary
passes for the members’ fair fiiends, had arranged
that every purchaser of a ticket should be entitled
to take one lady through the gate gratis. The
lady who won’t pay to come in had better come
in “ for nix ” on such occasions if she can induce a
casual ten-shilling man to bring her along.
* # #
An individual, who has long boasted that
he carries a “gun,” is said to have been pointing
it wildly at another person in a Sydney thorough-
fare, After the Laserou-Walker episode, and the
Redfern larrikin pistol affair, the police should
arrest every inebriate who is even suspected of
carrying firearms.

* m m
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Works
recently advertised for two junior clerks, announc-
ing at the same time that personal canvassing
would be regarded as a disqualification. The two
selected candidates were named Thwaites and
Gahan. Now, the Board’s engineer is a Mr.
Thwaites, and a Mr. Gahan is a member of the
Board. Curious coincidences !

days—is at least 50 per cent, in excess of the real
amount.
# * #
The Speight v. Age libel-action has been
again postponed. When the case does come on for
hearing, the defence, it is said, intends to apply for
a commission to take expert evidence in England
and America upon the question whether or not
Speight managed the Victorian railways pro-
perly. Meanwhile Richard wears his hat as
jauntily to the rear as aforetime, and thrives on
his leisure,
* # * .
Milner Stephen (brother to the N.S.W.
Lieutenant-Governor) is “creating a sensation”
at Melbourne, and “curing cancer by the mere
application of his fingers.” Hung up in Hunter-
street, Sydney, is the ugliest and heaviest and
most brutal-looking clump-shoe (an alleged
remnant of the Convict System, but more
likely a souvenir of the Blackwattle - swamp
navvies) that The Bulletin has ever seen. That
boot weighs say, four-and-a-half pounds, and is
bound with iron. The Bulletin will eat that
boot if Mr. Milner Stephen can cure, by the appli-
cation of his fingers, or by any other method short
of the knife, any one of a dozen cases of cancer
now in the Sydney hospitals. Mr. Milner
Stephen, or anyone else who pretends to cure
cancer, by the laying on of hands, is a humbug.
# * #
It is now asserted that Mr. Howler Ross
never put that famous wreath on Lindsay Gordon’s
grave at all; he only took it out of a brown-paper
parcel and held it in his hand while he recited a
dreary hunk of his own bad verse over the dead
poet, and then he packed up the offering and took
it away. Also, Mr. Ross, with his long hair flying
in the wind, and his “sombrero” trying to bolt
over the wall, wasn’t a pathetic spectacle, and the
cold breeze blew most of his poetry down his
throat, and his nose was red and he was obviously
agitated. There are only a very few men who can
look impressive as they stand with a bouquet upon
a grave ; the rest of them would look just about
as impressive if they stood with a turnip in a
circus.
* *
The daughter of an eminent Maoriland
Civil-servant got a bad set back recently. She
sent “Home” for household silver, &c., shortly
after her marriage, and through the kindness of a
friend in the Customs smuggled the goods into the
country. Her estimable father heard of this, and
though not be’onging to that department promptly
paid the full duty to the Customs and sent along
to his daughter’s husband the bill, together with a
lecture on the sinfulness of defrauding the “Guv*-
mint.”

Charters Towers (Q.) is one of the free-
and-easiest cities in Australia. Yon can, any time,
pass a man worth A 50,000 sitting on the kerbstone
with a friend, demonstrating the lay of a reef,
with his forefinger, in the gutter muck.

with embezzlement in Adelaide, received ABO a
year, was a married man, owned a racehorse, and
was a prominent figure at coursing and pony-
racing meetings.
# * #
Apropos of recent embezzlements in S.A.
public service, it is stated that influence was used
to cover up the deficiencies of a “ well-connected”
officer, whilst the others, who bad no friends,
were rounded up alongside the common burglar.
When it was found that the aristocratic em-
bezzler had lifted A5OO, he was allowed three days
to clear, and wisely cleared.
* # #
A very old woman, famished, rag-clad,
and penniless, was accused of vagrancy in Mel-
bourne, and the bench kindly offered to let her go
free “if she would promise not to repeat the
offence.” The irony of it! She was charged with
being poor and old and hungry and homeless, and
she was asked to promise that she would straighten
up and not be old and homeless any more. As
she didn’t see her way to manage it she got “three
months hard.”

* * *
A Melbourne detective, in cross-examina-
tion, has admitted receiving about AIOO in rewards
from fire-insurance companies for convictions for
arson. So far as The Bulletin can make out, the
average detective looks to the Government for
perhaps one-fourth of his income and to private
sources for the balance. To be found out in
undertaking private business should mean instant
dismissal from the force, as the prospect of private
“tip,” contingent on gaoling his game, involves
too great a strain on the veracity possessed by the
class of man who usually becomes a detective.
m m m
The connection between sensualism-gone-
mad and cold-blooded murder is apparently as
strong as that between ostentatious piety and the
coldest-blooded and most wholesale description of
fraud. The latest pair of accused Australian
poisoners were arrested while singing hymns.

Died of cheap imported literature —Mel-
bourne Ant. It was a good little publication, but
the English papers of the Bits order, which steal
their matter and their illustrations, and get their
paper at cheap English rates, and are worked by
155.-a-week clerks and sweated compositors, were
too much for it. The paper which pays for its
literary matter and its pictures generally gets left
in this way, just as the grocer who buys his sugar
is left by the one who prigs that substance and
embezzles the bags.

a (N.S.W.) country priest was recently burgled,
and among the articles removed were “ Two gold
bracelets (one horseshoe pattern, set with pearls,
the other horseshoe pattern), one gold brooch
(diamond in centre), one gold brooch (ruby in
centre), one gold wa ( cli chain (with gold cross
attached), one silver watch and chain, and 255. in
silver.” The Bulletin doesn’t want to cast
reflections on anybody, but when jewellery of this
sort is stolen from a holy father it sounds—well,
it sounds very like as if a cardinal had lost a lot
of lady’s underclothing.
* * *
The proceedings in a late N.S.W. slander
case became so monotonous that the presiding
Judge frequently—well, nodded. The case lasted
five days, so to relieve the tedium, games of
“sleep-to” were played in all parts of the court.
A pool was formed and each player made a guess
as to the number of minutes the Judge would
remain motionless during his next nap, the closest
guess to scoop the pool. During the course of one
pool his Honor preserved his immobility so far
beyond the period prescribed by the wildest
guesser that one solicitor openly wagered he
was dead. A certain lawyer-politician was at
that moment examining a witness when the oppos-
ing counsel rose to object to bis style of interroga-
tion. With a clownish leer the politician replied :
“ Object away, my learned friend, he ain’t listen-
ing.” The general laugh which followed this
retort awoke the Judge, who, to the poli-
tician’s disgust, ultimately sustained the objection
that had been urged against him.
Garrick, the Queensland Agent-General,
and Sir Charles Lilley were partners once as
solicitors. Now it is pretty hard for anyone with-
out a handle to his name to get near Garrick in
London. He has just been long enough away
from Australia to forget that his father drove a
baker’s cart, in Sydney, not so very long ago.

Many of the New Guinea tribes visited by
administrator M‘Gregor arc still in the stone age
of civilization. It is often the custom for a man
to exchange his sisters for some other chap’s
sisters, and make wives of them. The more sisters
the more wives.

THE YOUNG PIONEER.
(With apolo ies to Johann Seidl.)
’Neath the Torrid Sun of the Western plain
There Jieth a dear one dead :
O ! blue were the eyes and fair the curls
That clustered round his head.
There were none to smoothe his pillow down,
To close his eyelids dear ;
No sound of woman’s weeping fell,
There fell no burning tear ;
But far, where the little homestead lies,
An old man’s hair is grey ;
Ills heart is faint with a deadly fear
For the son so far away.
And the mother wails her absent one
As Rachel did of old—
"No more I’ll see his eyes of blue
Or dress his locks of gold ”
While a winsome maiden bows her head,
As the big brown eyes run o’er—
“ And though my love lies low in death,
I will love him evermore.”
And the tears that fast and faster fall.
The kind Heaven lifts on high,
And forms of the drops a cloudlet pale,
That floats to the western sky.
And the tears fall soft from the cloudlet down,
Afar on t he dear one’s head ;
That he lie not alone on the Western plain,
Unwept—among the dead.
Q.
P. L.
Our father’s dead. Alas ! ’twas willed ;
The erring doctors could not save.
“ Ml haunt ye,” said he, “ if ye build
A German tombstone o’er my grave.”

A newspaper correspondent in a touching
letter re the destruction of marsupials, exhibits
the tendency common to a large class, towards
employing conventional phrases u ithont respect to
their appropriateness. “ Young and old, mothers
and joeys,” he writes patlu t'cally, “all, all fall
silent vie ims before his murderous rifle.” Ho
teems to regard it as an aggravation of the busi-
ness that the kangaroos are not afforded an oppor-
tunity for making a last dying speech, and saying
that it was the accursed drink that brought them
to this.

In Defence of the Bush.
(Os Reading Henky Lawson’s “ Borderland .”)
g 0 you’re back from up tho country, Mister Law-
son, where you went,
And you’re cursing all the business in a bitter
discontent;
Well, we grieve to disappoint you, and it makes
us sad to hear
That it wasn’t cool and shady—and there wasn’t
plenty beer,
4nd the loony bullock snorted when you first came
into view ;
Well, you know, it’s not so often that he sees a
swell like you;
And the roads were hot and dusty, and the plains
were burnt and brown,
And no doubt you’re better suited drinking lemon-
squash in town.
y e t, perchance, if you should journey down the
very track you went
]n a month or two at furthest you would wonder
what it meant,
Where the sunbaked earth was gasping like a
creature in its pain
You would find the, grasses waving like a field of
summer grain,
And the miles of thirsty gutters blocked with sand
and choked with mud,
You would find them mighty rivers with a turbid,
sweeping flood ;
For the rain and drought and sunshine make no
changes in the street,
In the sullen line of buildings and the ceaseless
tramp of feet;
J3ut the bush hath moods and changes, as the
seasons rise and fall,
And tlie men who know the bush-land-they are
loyal through it all.
But you found the bush was dismal and a land of
no delight,
Did you chance to hear a chorus in the shearers’
huts at night?
Did they “rise up, William Riley,” by the camp-
fire’s cheery blaze ?
Did they rise him as we rose him in the good old
droving days ?
And the women of the homesteads and the men
you chanced to meet—
Were their faces sour and saddened like your
“ faces in the street,”
And the “shy selector children ” —were they better
now or worse
Than the city urchins mentioned who would greet
you wish a curse ?
Is not such a life much better than the squalid
street and square
Where the fallen women flaunt it in the fierce
electric glare,
Where the sempstress plies her sewing till her eye*
are sore and red
In a filthy, dirty attic toiling on for daily bread ?
Did you hear no sweeter voices in the music of the
bush
Than the roar of trams and ’busses and the war-
whoop of “ the push ; ”
Did the magpies rouse your slumbers with their
carol sweet and strange?
Did you hear the silver chiming of the bell-birds
on the range ?
But, perchance, the wild birds’ music by your
senses was despised.
For you say you’ll stay in townships till tho bush
is civilised.
Would you make it a tea-garden and on Sundays
have a band
Where the “ blokes ” might take their “ donahs ”
with a “ public ” close at hand ?
You had better stick to Sydney and make merry
with “ the push,”
For the bush will never suit you, and you’ll never
suit the bush.

Dear Bulletin, —A pighunt in Maoriland
is one of the best sports going. Boar-hunting (as
it is magniloquently called) in the Black Forest is
a fool to it. There you stand with your “ loader,”
if you choose, in a fairly-open forest and piggy is
driven to you, and with delicately-sighted
express, or perhaps still more deadly chokebore
and buckshot, you wait a fair shot in perfect
safety, and bowl him over at your ease. Never-
theless, when the execution is done, you are hailed
a Nimrod. One gets different work, if in company
only with a couple of good dogs and a sheathknife
he goes into some of the black gullies of the
Mauawatu. Scrambling through the tangle
of supplejack and vines, your dogs presently
get scent, and, led by their music, you find
them bailing up an old “Captain Cooker’—one
of those thin-flanked, high-backed, bristly old
devils, lie is probably in the creek-bed, and you
must get behind him and, dexterously catching
hold of his hind foot, throw him over, and the
knife soon settles matters. It's awkward if the
dogs don't hold, because somebody generally gets
hurt—though it’s mostly the dogs—for whose
Repairs one generally carries a needle and thread.
| saw a nasty thing one day in the Wairarapn,
Hiding with a couple of young Maori!anders and
a new-chum, the dogs stuck up a pig in a neigh-
bouring gully, and new-chum begged to be allowed
fo go and kill the pig solus. Oft he went, and as
be didn’t return in a reasonable time we went
down—to find him in a dead faint with his
groin ripped open. It appears the pig was
m a hole in the creek-bed, which was only
a couple of feet wide, and couldn’t be got
at, so chummy straddled across the banks
and heaved the biggest rock he could find down
( >n the pork below. Bank gives way, down comes
J'bummy, and exit pig leaving his mark cn route.
l jm funniest thing I ever saw pig-hunting was
, lere a large Newfoundland dog got out of the
•losebox in which he had been looked and followed
ms master into the bush, arriving just as a frir-
sized old pig was started. In rushed Nelson
ahead of all the other dogs and s mod piggy by
be chops, and hung on, shaking the pig like a
.bit. We expected to sec the, top of his head
upped off—but, no -still lie hung there, and on
I "'8 fbe pig we found the reason why. The
,i oar bad only one tusk and the dog had got him on
le gummy side. The remaining tusk was curled
'o.npletely round through, the top grinder being
f 'me, and formed a perfect ring and a bit over.
°n old Jackson’s mantel-shelf now. Yours,

More physicians commit suicide than
members of any other of the learned professions.
W hen a man has learned to be a physician he has
acquired so much horrible information that he has
to die to get rid of it.

Wi Waka, a noble savage, intended by
nature for a political economist, has just died in
the Wairarapa, Maoriland. Once he had sold on
behalf of his tribe a lai'ge tract of land, and it was
agreed that the proceeds should be evenly divided
between chief and people. Wi converted the
price into ill-notes, had all his people squatted
round him in a ring, and proceeded to “divide;”
To the first man he gave a note, putting at the.
same time another into his own pocket by way of
“ equal division,” With the second man he did
the same, and so on, round and round the ring,
till it was all “ divided.” The tribe was satisfied
—so was Wi Waka. Which reminds one of the
comparatively crude methods of the Irish gentle-
man who, when Commissioned to divide four
pounds equally between three people—himself
and two others —said : “ Here's two for yon two
and two for me too.”

Dear Bulletin, — When the female suff-
rage debate arose in Maoriland Parliament, the
other day, a hostile amendment proposing to
allow women seats in the House of Representa-
tives was defeated by only 31 to 21 votes. Logi-
cally speaking, woman is just as fairly entitled to
jostle with man in the House as at the polling-
booth, but the desirableness or otherwise of
Women’s Suffrage is a matter readily disposed of.
It is contended by the democratic parties that
given the ono-man-one-woman-one-vote system the
preponderance of progressive voting-power will
fall into their hands, but seeing that (judging
from recent experiments in America) woman’s
tendency is to vote directly against her male
relatives’ interest, and support unreservedly any
smug mediocrity who travels solely on liquor-
abolition, Bible in State schools, freetrade in
latest Paris fashions, or London penny-
dreadful advocacy, the assumed advantages
derivable from the extension of the suffrage
to the opposite sex become very doubtful. The
relative proportions of males and females being
.about equal in Australia, it is hard to see why
woman should not prefer to delegate her nearest
male relation to carry out her views in the rough
arenas of constitutional legislation instead of
denuding herself of the privileges which at present
attend her actions, for the tinsel glory of active
participation in the struggle for existence with
man on equal grounds. The men who have
hitherto ruled this country have by no means
legislated against their own women folk, but
rather have specially hedged them in against the
possible encroachments of “common people.”
When the Democratic party (at present by no
means fully enfranchised, while their opponents
are over-enfranchised) secures a majority in the
legislative institutions of the country, it also will
enact for the protection of its female associates,
but when doing this it will carefully avoid placing
class disabilities upon women of a different social
status to its own. Taking all things into con-
sideration, woman has nothing to fear from man’s
monopoly of the exercise of Parliamentary func-
tions, and man, so far, has nothing to gain by
a change from present arrangements.—Yours,
CHARLES JUNON.

WILLIAM JOHN LYNE.
What a spectacle for the beggardom of
Europe must be that excursion mob of 47 American
millionaires now on tour through Europe ! These
vulgar advertisers of their own sordidness and
rapacity deserve to fall into the hands of banditti
Who would demand all their millions in ransom
for their shins.
Boots’ Indigestion Cure, a purely Austra-
lian preparation which has met with much success
in Australia during the comparatively short time
it has been before the public, is now successfully
invading the London market, a large shipment
having been sent “home” to cure the deranged
stomach and enlarged liver of J. Bull. Judging
by the results obtained in Australia, this is a good
thing for the said Bull.
ZTIu these days of social unrest it is hard to
say which will be the outcome—evolution or
rfevolutiori. One thing we know unceasing
change is Nature’s jaw. Whatever may be the
result of the world-wide rows between Labour and
Capital, every man and woman possessed of
wealth should keep their securities safe from fires
and buglars in the Sydney Safe Deposit—under
their own lock and key—avoiding the necessity
for trusting anyone. *
A London writer: “ Our ideas of right
and wrong are largely conventional. To an
Oriental a fashionable London ball-room is one of
the obsoenest sights imaginable, and in Mahom-
ttledan countries a modest woman on the approach
bf a stronger Will lift her only garment in order
td hide her face. It is riot the nude that shocks
the ordinary mind, but the undressed, so that ail
artist may produce a really offensive picture
(while satisfying the canons of art as laid
down by the solicitor’s clerk in a recent
prosecution) by painting his heroine in her
flannel petticoat. A workman may pass through
the streets of London in his shirt-sleeves un-
noticed, but a gentleman who did so, or who,
without going to this extreme, chose to walk
about with his coat-tails tucked up under his arm,
would probably attract the attention of the
police) and rightly so. In all such matters the
ruling principle. Which the Chadbands of Society
overlook, is the association of ideas. ”

Allt's Brewing and Wine and Spirit Co.
(Ltd.), of Charlotte Place, Sydney, have been
appointed sole agents for James Watson and Co.’s
Dundee whisky, and are placing a large quantity
of that soul-stirring beverage on the market. It
is a pale, straw-coloured drink, with an insinuating
odour like a candidate’s smile, and a reflected
glitter like our best girl’s deep, brown eyes, and a
taste like the liquors which some angel will mix in
Paradise. Also there is inspiration at the bottom
of the bottle, and many a man who put his savings
in a land-bank and has been sold up for calls
Would have done better if he had invested the
whole lot in Watson’s whisky, and had one great,
soul-satisfying burst, and th6n lived on the
memory of it for ever after. Economy isn’t the
virtue it is cracked up to be. The thriftless
individual at least has the liquor to look back
upon, while the other fellow saves his money and
puts it in a building society, and when the affair
bursts in his old age he goes mad with the shock,
and wanders about with a dishevelled eye and
straws in his hair, while the man who stole his
little pile gets a “ vote for thrift.”
Sheep cases. Boundary-rider in box.
Exhausted Q.C. recoiling in despair from impreg-
nable density of witness: “Now we’ll get some-
thing out of you. What’s the station ear-marks ?”
“ Block out and two notches.” Interval of 10
minutes. Happy thought strikes counsel. Takes
out pen-knife, cuts excellent model of sheep’s ear
out of blotting-sheet; hands to witness with knife
and exclaims beamingly: “Now then, here you
are—please cut out on this pattern sheep’s ear
your block-out and two notches.” Witness studies
model attentively, examines it upside and down-
side ; puts it down in despair. Counsel wrath-
fully: “Well, what’s the matter —hurry up!”
Witness attacks the model again—more study.
Counsel: “ Come on, man, what’s the matter
with your mark ? Don’t you know it ?” Witness,
slowly ; “Which end is the butt of this ’ere ear?”
Counsel indicates the butt. Witness slowly and
with infinite care cuts out the block-out; hands
model to Q.C. with easy confidence of successful
artist. Counsel: “Yes; well, that’s the block-
out —what’s the matter with the notches V Wit-
ness : “Yes, that’s the block-out right enough.”
Counsel, snappishly : “ Well, where are the
notches?” Witness, calmly : “On the other ear.”
To those who knew the late Charles
Keene, unquestionably the most accomplished
artist that was ever on the staff of London Punch,
the idea of a “life” of him being published is
eminently absurd. Yet such a book has just been
issued, and very interesting reading it is, mainly
on account of the admirable letters it contains.
Keene was always a Bohemian of the Bohemians,
and “Society” knew him not. The Punch
weekly dinners he voted “stodgy,” and even
when he had a right to attend them he seldom
availed himself of the privilege. When he was
seen at his best was at the Friday nights of the
Langham Sketching Club, a free and easy assembly
of working artists, where an entertainment that
would scarcely be licensed by the Lord Chamber-
lain used to be periodically given. His tall, gaunt
figure, surmounted by a head that reminded you of
an ideal Don Quixote, was seldom absent on these
occasions, and the present writer can testify
to Keene’s ever-willing readiness to give advice to
younger men—advice that was always of the
supremest value. Fat billets make fat men, but
Keene, although ho left behind him some .£37,000
in the way of personalty, was an exception to this
rule, and thus afforded a striking contrast to Ids
fellow-worker on Punch. Mark Lemon, the first
editor, played Falstaff without stuffing ; Shirley
Brooks, Frank Burnand, Harry Furniss and
Liuley Sambourne, were, and are, stout men, per-
haps the result of the banquets which Keene
shunned. It may be mentioned that the artist’s
powers were never properly recognised in Eng-
land, but in France, where good work in appre-
ciated, he was considered a master in black-ami
whits, an opinion that will be thoroughly endorsed
by anybody who knows anything of the subject.
In view of the recent reductions in the wages
of the Sydney compositors (ostensibly because of the
“financial depression”), it is interesting to note that
Sydney D.T. proprietary lias just declared (or is about to
declare) a dividend of 15 per cent.

A fanner writes to the Age complaining of the
wrought t. fen es and crops by the “rich squires”
~f Melbourne Hunt Chib when chasing lame deer (the
same bid tame deer every fine) over bis bit of land. It
the fanner knew the exact social status of some people
who cut. up his turnip field he would probably growl still
m ire. A prominent “ squire ”of the M. 11.0. is an in-
solvent lint tipster,.with whom Lord Hopetoun innocently
compares notes on their respective bunting-togs and to
whom his lordship might well suggest the advisable ness
of paying something in the A! at the earliest possible date
to bis anxious creditors.

It doesn't take much to constitute a “ groat
(•vent" in the estimation of an athletic editor. Melbourne
Si’jbts.man, after bewailing the meagre attendance (of
deadheads, chiefly) at a recent wrestling-match, devoted
two columns of space to the petty details of the unin-
teresting business. It is solely on account of the lament-
able publicity and bogus importance given to these linpot
contests by the sporting press that “champions,” “ ex-
champions,” “coining champions,” and the handful of
Barneys, Ikes, and so on. who “look after them in their
irork" are able to drink themselves to death at the
expense of young Jugginses when they ought to be carry-
ing bricks.

In the evening papers on the Friday before
Grand National, Astronomer Ellery advised people to
bring their umbrellas and overcoats with them. As usual,
it turned out fine and sunny, though cold. However,
Ellery went at near as most of the sporting tipsters did to
the National winner.
# * #
There was a man in Maoriland who bonneted
Slav in and lived to die in his bed—of had whisky. He was
a rackety ex-University man, and heard that Slavin was
at a Wellington hotel. He went there, saw a stalwart
individual in a tall hat, and said: “Mr. Slavin, 1 believe?”
“Yes.” “Then take that, Mr. Slavin V’—that being a
blow on the tall hat which knocked it over the wearer’s
eves. The fighter got out of his hat and the spectators
held their breaths, expecting to figure at an early inquest,
but, after eyeing his assailant a moment, Slavin merely
straightened out his concertina’d tile and sat down with-
out a”word. Next day, the assailant sobered up, made
ample apology, stood a new hat, and they were fast
friends after.

Dear Bulletin,—What’s up with Jack Hempton
and the rest of the Maoriland boys in England—is it the
climate, or what ? Jack could always do the 100yds. in
evens at any time during the last five years, and I have
myself timed him 9 4-sths on several occasions during
practice, and once 9 3-sths, tho’ this last I will not swear
to-so hard to get your clock away with the pistol when
you are at the other end. Batger, too, is another sur-
prise, as he was yards too good for anybody else in Maori-
land, and has, I believe, a record of 16 l-sth for 120yds.
hurdles, I remember travelling with Hempton, a few
years back, down from Palmerston North to Wellington,
when he had just won eight races in three days (all he
started for) from scratch, and had put in the evenings
. dancing, with a little “ Hokitika” afterwards. Can he he
the same man now? It’s perhaps news to yon that
Hempton never was strong enough to stand hard training,
and won most of his M.L. races without any but the
slightest preparation.—Yours, Toruiiaxa.

Near Stockton (N.S.Vv.) is an old cemetery un-
enclosed and close to the road. Only two graves remain,
one with tombstone intact. A little way off is the cricket-
ground, and the ambition of the players is to put the
leather over the fence into the cemetery. Such a bit is
called a “ tomber ”—it scores six, and a reward like the
“ hat-trick.”

W. J. Franks, better known in Sydney as
“ Yorkshireman” and the “Turf Attorney-General,”
died of asthma on Saturday last at Surry Hills. He had
been a political power in his day, in England, was a very
well-read man and a most pronounced agnostic. He had
long been obliged to depute his outdoor work to his sons.
# # *
In tipping Rerlleap last week The Bulletin
committed itself to the statement that Mr. Miller’s horse
was the Carbine of the “ sticks.” Redicap fully justified
this title by winning the V.R.C. Grand National Steeple-
chase with the biggest weight on record in the quickest
time on record. Only the South Australian nag Confidence
had a ghost of a show with the champion after they had
gone two miles and a-hnlf, and when it came to racing
down the straight, Confidence was badly left. This was
indeed one of those good things which are worth tipping
in a newspaper, when you know a’'out it. The Bulletin
happened to know enough to think that Redleap was
certain-as racing certainties go —to win on a sound
course, and therefore we “ gave it to ’em straight.”
At Poverty Point.
"Word of an old friend comes from Liverpool (Eng.). A
local music-hall notice says that “dainty, charming,
vivacious Amy Horton is a genuine, good all-round turn.”
Amy still sports a long pinafore and a lisp, we presume.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent says that Buffalo
Bill has imported a lot of Oossacka to add some novelty
to his Wild West Show revival, which is very monotonous.
He needs some good Australian rough-riders to show
what can he done in the way of riding buokjumpers in
naked saddles, not in great things like armchairs, such as
the over rated cowboys use. There is not a genuine
buckjumper in the whole of Bill’s stud. His horses only
pig-jump.
Atkinson, “the Australian Orpheus,” is a mighty hit
at London Trocadero. Imitates musical instruments,
sings a magnificent soprano, and has even sung before
Queen Victoria.
The Melbourne Sunday concerts go on in the most dar-
ing style. At first there was a mock reverence, but now
the applause and encores are loud and furious, and the
music is nearly all secular, for the sacred goes a long way.
Although the London representative of Joan of Arc
wore male habiliments, Marion Hood never will.
It seems to be the policy of the Gaiety Co. stage-
management to ignore the popular demand for more
Alice Leamar (who is probably first favourite amongst
the girls of the crowd), for the reason, perhaps, that the
lively damsel in question hasn’t the remotest connection
with the Gaiety Co. in London.
C. H. Burford, the veteran Australian actor who now
appeals to his brother pros, for help, appeared at the age
of 18 with Macready, when the latter took his farewell at
Liverpool.
There was a large Australian contingent at London
Shaftesbury at the premiere of “ Hero and Leander.”
Mrs. Dalglish’s party filled almost a row of stalls.
A Maitland (N.S.W,)yarn has been resurrected, con-
cerning the tremendous appetite possessed by the late
Yamdaveda, who, in ’76, went to Maitland as “contor-
tionist” with the Keller-Linglook il'usionists. They
“hung out” at the Royal, then kept by one Hodgson.
Dinner was “ on ” one Sunday, turkey at one end and a
fine sucking-pig at the other. Hodgson tackled the
carving of the'turkey, and Yamdaveda was told off to
carve the sucker. Nobody took much notice of him, till,
just as Hodgson had carved for himself, Yamdaveda said,
“ Mr. Hodgson, have you any more of these little hogs? ”
He had eaten the whole pig himself.
A Sydney pianiste now in Maoriland bearded the local
Ministry in their dens and cornered them into buying
tickets for her ben. Result: “ Notice to canvassers. Can-
vassing in these buildings for the sale of goods, tickets
for entertainments, or any similar purpose, cannot be
allowed. By order. June, 1892.” The sight of a
mysterious female in the passages is now enough to upset
a whole Cabinet.

managers took him for a soubrette, on account of the
“ Eille,” and when he arrived he found a waiting-maid’s

At one of the London music halls, recently, there was a
big benefit for the widow and child of somebody named
Pleon, and the feature of the occasion was “ an address
written by Richard Morton, and delivered by Ben Nathan
in a most feeling manner.” The benefit committee and
all the artistes who had given their services stood around
the stage in a semi-circle whilst the affecting address was
recited. Here are two verses:—
To you, dear friends, who’ve rallied here to-day,
I have a message of few’ words to say.
1 have to tender now the deep and true
Thanks of a widow’ and a child to you.
To one and all, you—by your presence here—
Drove that a good cause to you all is dear,
And by your firm support strive to express
Your sympathy with one in loneliness.
The music-hall bard, alike in his gay and serious moods,
seems to be the saddest tiling on this earth.

when ho ran the Devenport (Eng.) Theatre Royal :
On this evening will ho presented

“ Other characters by the company ” is good.
In a Viennese waxworks is shown a representation of
“The Last Moments of the Czar of Russia,” thus
described The Czar, modelled to the life, lies on a
real bed, with his unilorm and his linen saturated with
Mood. His shirt is open to the breast, and a horrible
gaping wound is exposed to the eye of the spectator. As
the figure breathes convulsively, the eyes roll up in the
head,'and real Mood gushes up to the surface of the
terrible wound. I have never been so horribly fascinated
in my life as I was by that figure of the dying C;esar, with
his life-blood welling up before my eyes.”

The best-off actor is the one who can play old men from
20 years of age up to 70, like Barren. In England there
used to be a young man who acted Sir Anthony Absolute,
in “ The Rivals,” with his own father as his son, Captain
Absolute.
A play-goer left Christchurch (M.L.) Royal between
acts to “see a man,” and while he was out a chunk of
ceiling fell and smashed his chair. Providence guards
him who goes out to see a man.

Dear Bulletin,—l submit that the mere fact that a
lady is popular and beautiful and can sing a bird off a tree
is no reason why the committee for her ben. should des-
patch a pound’s worth of tickets to all the professional
and business men of a city with a polite intimation that if
not. sold they will be charged for unless returned to some-
body.—J. Know.

The question now before English critics: Who is the
leading actor of to-day, Irving or Beerbohm Tree? The
latter’s supporters aver him to be the more versatile, by
far.

Gracie Plaisted, who died the other day in America,
will be remembered here as a bright and vivacious actress,
with a fairly good voice. Her most successful part was in
“La Perichole,” in which her drunken song, “I am so
tight,” was a marvellous piece of realistic work. She also
tried to put some life into Luscombe Searelle’s wonderful
operatic effusions, but that was a task beyond her powers
—or anybody else’s.

Sydney Supreme Court the other day
afforded an instance of a defendant actually
appearing for plaintiff. But there the anomaly
ends, for it was only Bruce Smith holding a brief
for the Burwood Land Co. in the action against
himself as Minister for Works, nominal defendant.
This shows the absurdity in practice of Judge
Windeyer’s tirade against a certain gallant officer
for not attending and interesting himself in
a certain case after the Government had
honoured him with the position of nominal defen-
dant. The nominal defendant that day preferred
the lawn at Rand wick.

A little sum : Rabbits begin to breed when
four months old. The period of gestation is 21
days, the litter is from G to 11 in number ; the
period of rest after motherhood varies from a
week to a fortnight, it is said. If Bobolink turned
out a capable young male and female rabbit on Ist
April, 1802, and this pair regularly every 6§ weeks
reared a household of 3 males and 4 females, alter-
nately with 4 males and 3 females ; and every
descendant pair followed the same conservative
rule—then, how long would it be before Mr. and
Mrs. Initial Bunny owned a progeny of 1,741,824?
The answer should come out exactly and without
fractions, or it is wrong.

Walt er Bcsant, the English novelist, and
apparently one of the sinoerest democrats who
draws breath, on the sweating system :
In all the miserable tales of sweating that we have
heard, there is nothing more miserable and disheartening
than the story of the publishing department of the
Society for Promoting Christian Knowledge.
And then we have a mass of ghastly detail about
long hours and exhausting labour in poisonous
dens, of starving, rag-clad children driven day
and night by the hard taskmasters of the Society
for the Promotion of Christian Knowledge, of
girls nearly dying of exhaustion in preparing
cheap tracts for the heathen, of preachers grinding
out the lives of the poor to get up good books
about hell for the savage on the Juruay River,
of women driven to immorality to eke out the
miserable pittance which they receive for the
eternal stitching of pamphlets concerning the
Bread of Life, and of fierce snuffiebnsters urging
on hungry, bony employes at Hid. a day in order
to bring out more books fur the Carib and the
Cherokee about the Lord who had pity upon the
poor. Sweating is a fine art with the Society for
the Promotion of Christian Knowledge. Bell !
this world is hell.

One week, long agoiie, when the Prince
of Wales was supposed to be dying and drapers
were selling their black stuffs at fancy prices,
Tenniel prepared two cartoons for London Punch,
The first picture, entitled “Suspense,”represented
the British nation in a broken-up condition wait-
ing for its beloved Prince to die. The alternative
cartoon represented Britannia weeping for the
royal corpse. Both were engraved, and up to the
very moment of going to press the editor was
prepared to chuck out “ Suspense”in favour of the
grief-stricken female who symbolized a bereaved
nation in its first agonies. The Prince lived on,
as everybody is aware, and so Tenniel’s second-
string was put away in the engraver’s desk and
forgotten for thirteen happy years. Then all
unexpectedly and of a sudden, the Duke of
Albany died, and Britannia had to weep before
there was time for Punch to get a picture drawn.
Whereupon the engraver went sadly to his desk,
saying “everything comes to the loyal cartoon
that waits ” and the figure that had anticipated
the decease of Albert Edward appeared as in the
up to-date act of going frantic over the loss of his
brother. And this suggestion of the nation being
knocked acock by the death of Queen Victoria’s
eighth child—a poor rickety princeling who fell a
victim to his first mild fit of hilariousness—was
the nearest approach to a good joke that the
present generation of cockneys has seen in Punch.
To Eat Well, to Sleep Well, to Work Well, to
Feel Well —Take Wolfe's Schnapps.

Chops!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There is a chop to be found in the dry-
hash-house, and this chop is a much-travelled one.
Originally it was mutton. The greasy part had
been removed, and the chop had then been grilled
by an expert for a dandy in a leading pub. After
the cook had served it up with salt, pepper, and
other condiments, it was set before the guest. But
bis stomach, frizzed to death after a terrible orgy,
couldn’t look at it. Outside, a barefooted boy
gazed, with yearning eyes, at the plenteous supply
within. The whole refuse of the breakfast-table
was then sold to the keeper of a restaurant in
Bourke-street. The proprietor had the chop pre-
pared for himself; but, having over-gorged on
trotters, it escaped. The remains of this estab-
lishment were carried, as per agreement, to a
boardinghouse on the wharf. The chop was set
apart for “ the missus,” but, better provender turn-
j n tr up, it was discarded. It next found its way into
the advertised boarding-bouse, with cheap Chinese
vegetables laid on, in Lonsdale-strect. ft is now
0 f a sickly whiteness, and is ready for the brain-
worker to eat.
* * *
A young man enters the city. He lives
on sixpenny feeds to make his few shillings last out.
He can get no work. He begins to write for the
papers. He has for breakfast, one morning, the
above-mentioned chop. It does not give his brain
tissue one particle of phosphate to recoup loss
caused by fretting; and presently the lad blows
out his brains. So goes a fond mother’s son. For
the benefit of the jury at the inquest, it is here
stated that the cause of death was the receipt of
lion-nutritions food by the stomach of the now-
silent young fellow; and also that, under such
conditions, cannibalism should be a virtue. I
know a beefy subject in the vicinity.
POLYPE.
To Readers of “The Bulletin,”
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains lias at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to Thß
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by Tiik Bulletin ; 1360 words ao to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the batter Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column w ill do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.

(Vic.)
Nothing Justified a man shooting at another, unless it
was to prevent a felony, or in an extreme case of self-
defence, or when a burglar was found in the house.
Broputty, proputty ! 11 may bo a common bur-
glary and you can shoot on sight, but only in
extreme cases of self-defence.
J. L. Dow, speaking on behalf of the Mel-
bourne printing-trade, has extracted from the
Vie. Minister of Customs a promise that he will
lend his official ear to any possible scheme for
cheeking the wholesale importation of penny
“comic” papers of the Bits tribe. The thing
can be easily done. At present a number of these
rags are sold under false colours as “ Australian
editions” when they are not even set up in this
country, and they ought to be as much open to
seizure as Australian hats, or bottles of Lager
Beer which pretend to have come from France
and Germany respectively. But the proper person
to deal with this matter is the Minister of Educa-
tion. A Bill for the Protection of Children
against British illustrated “ humour ” would
meet the case.
In all the large-sized town of West and
East Maitland, N.S.W., there is not a single
pawnshop. Maitland consists of one street, about
two miles long, and a lot of side-alleys leading 1 to
nowhere. It is bounded by lucerne patches and
Chinese gardens, but the principal vegetation is
that of the shopkeepers, whose whole duty in life
is to “dress ” their windows 73 times a day, and
try and think hard what a sovereign is like. No
tradesman, except ttjp progman and the grogman,
ever has a customer, and he does not seem to
expect one. He just lives on, benzines his clothes,
waits for his bank-account to wear out, and then
sells out to some foreign fool who has heard that
Maitland is an old-fashioned, solid town. The
most contented residents are in its gaol. Bullock-
teams are common sights in its streets, but the
stranger is liable to mistake the miserable crawlers
(it takes 12 to move a ton) for goats. A joey-nute
man, the other day, had no difficulty in passing
several forged “livers,” purporting to be on the
BankofN.S. Wales, the receivers being so over-
come with emotion that they were on the point of
getting up a banquet in,honor of the opulent visitor
when the forgeries were discovered, and a peni-
tential meeting was held instead. Even the
publicans are dejected. Swaggie doesn’t get half
drunk enough now, and Papercollar patronises
°nly the threepenny bar. Maitland has architects
whose clerks never draw anything but one leg
after the other ; solicitors whose whole business is
connected with wills, mortgages, loans, &c. ;
doctors who are only needed when the mid-
wife is ; and chemists whose most serious
trade rivals are the barbers. The town
has magnificent banks which you hardly ever
■see auyboding entering. The best-looking town
house is the lawn-fronted, trim-hedged, godly
parsonage, that smugs on complacently in contrast
to hundreds of battered, vermin-infested hovels
°* the Noah’s Ark style of architecture. Any
stranger_ who enters a Maitland hotel and
shouts •’ for “ all in the bar ” at once secures the
tnendship for life of that landlord, and runs the
'’■sk of being waited on bv a deputation of
homage, headed by the Mayor and the local bill
poster. But ask a Maitland tradesman to buy
Anything and ho vvili assassinate you.

JN FAVOUR OF MOTHERS-IN-LAW.
Perhaps one of the best-abused individuals
under what Mrs. Partington would designate the
“blue canister of heaven ” is the lady who attains
to membership in the distinguished order of
mothers-in law. Why it is, however, that she,
above all others of her sex, should be singled out
for ridicule and coarse jest, is about as difficult
to understand as is the wisdom of Providence in
permitting otherwise peaceful bedchambers to be
turned into haunts of wretchedness and profanity
through the existence of the bloodthirsty vagrant
mosquito. What dreadful crime has the unfortu-
nate woman committed in providing the man with
his wife that he should bear her such an undying
grudge ? When the young couple first enter upon
the mysteries of housekeeping, who is it that
comes along and with her practical experience
and good sense shows them an easy way over
rough places? The man’s mother in-law. And
when the first little pledge of affection makes
its appearance, as well as the successive ones,
who is it that steps in and relieves the
husband's anxiety, and takes the load of care
and worry off the wife’s feeble shoulders, and
keeps the household machinery running smoothly ?
Why, the man’s mother in-law. For all of which
devotion he rewards her by making heartless jokes
at her expense, and publishing them for other
men to snigger over ! Ingratitude, thy name is
man ! Mothers-in-law are not what the satirist
has painted them. This is a petrified fact that
cannot be too forcibly rammed into the receptive
apparatus of the average young benedict who
somehow gets away with the notion that the
world without his presence in it would be a
pronounced failure. The sum of human know-
ledge is ever on the increase. For example, at one
period the rheumatic sufferer held the belief that,
apart from medical skill directly applied iu the
person of one of the “ duly qualified,” there was
no relief for his malady. He has since grown
wiser in his generation ; and now when the
twinges of some muscular disease denote the
approaching attack, he invests a modest half-
crown upon a bottle of St. .Jacobs Oil, and van-
quishes the unwelcome foe at the inception of the
strife. With a supply of this truly invaluable
pain conqueror in the house, anyone may be his
or her own physician in curing nearly every de-
scription of accidental injury, as well as in eradi-
cating the pains occasioned by neuralgia, rheuma-
tism, gout, backache, toothache, etc., and ulti-
mately putting to flight the prevailing cause of
such suffering.
