The Drover’s Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The “ house ” contains two rooms ; is built
of round timber - , slabs, and stringy-bark, and
floored with split slabs. A big bark kitchen
stands at the end, and is larger than the house,
itself, verandah included.
Bush all round —.bush with no horizon, for the
country is flat. No ranges in the distance. The
hush consists of stunted, rotten “native apple
trees.” No undergrowth. Nothing to relieve the
eye, save the darker green of a few she oaks which
are sighing above the narrow, almost waterless
creek. Nineteen miles to the nearest civilisation
a shanty on the main road.
!he drover—an ex-squatter—is away with sheep.
His wife and children are left here alone. ,
Four ragged, dried up-looking children are play-
ing about the house. Suddenly one of them yells:
Snake ! Mother, here’s a snake !”
I he gaunt, sun-browned bushwoman darts from
the kitchen, snatches “the baby” from the
ground, holds it on her left hip, and reaches for a
stick.
“ Where is it ?”
“Here! gone into the wood-heap!” yells the
eldest boy—a sharp-faced, excited urchin of eleven.
‘Stop there, mother!’ I’ll have the !” (he
swears like a trooper) “ I’ll have the !”
‘Tommy, come here, or you’ll be bit. Come
here at once when I tell you, you little wretch !
“ ~ you (that I should say such a thing!', come
e * e • ’ s ho shrieks. Her voice is not very musical.
The youngster swears beneath his breath, and
comes reluctantly, carrying a stick nearly as big
a * himself. Suddenly he yells, triumphantly :
I here it goes, under the house!” and darts
away, with club uplifted. At the same time, the
'g, black, yellow-eyed dog-of-all-breeds, who has
f'°' vn the greatest interest in the proceedings,
breaks his chain and darts after the snake. He is
a moment late, however, and his nose reaches the
crack in the slabs just as the end of the snake’s
ai disappears. Almost at the same moment the
°y s dub comes down and skins the aforesaid
nos e- The dog takes small notice of this and pro-
ceeds to undermine the building; but he is sub-
„!f c a struggle and chained up. They can’t
aff ord to lose him.
c^rovprs makes the children stand
ler near the dog-house while she watches for
se't ®be ffets two small dishes of milk and
out • l'° m own near Jr 6 wall to tempt the snake
itself Ut an k° ur £ ocs by and it does not show
Tli! 'r • I ? 6ar sun set, and a thunderstorm is coming.
uoM't- en mua k be brought inside. She will
snake ° ( ' l6m * nt ° t ' lo bouse, for she knows the
thro n-T lere anc l may at any moment come up
she crac b s ' n the rough slab floor. So
carries several armfuls of firewood into the
kitchen, and then takes the children there. The
kitchen has “no floor,” or rather an earthen one
called a “ground floor ” in this part of the bush.
There is a large, roughly-made table in the centre
of the place. She brings the children in and
makes them get on this table. They are two boys
and two girls—mere babies. She gives them some
supper, and then, before it gets dark, she goes
into the house, and snatches up some pillows and
bed-clothes—expecting to see or lay her hand on
the snake any minute. She makes a bed on the
kitchen-table for the children and sits down beside
it to watch all night,
She has an eye on the corner, and a green sap-
bug chib laid in readiness on the dresser by her
side; also her sewing basket, and a copy of the
Young Ladies’Journal. She has brought the
dog into the room.
Tommy turns in under protest, and says he’ll
lay awake all night and smash that nameless
snake,
His mother asks him how many times she has
told him not to swear.
He has his club with him under the bed-clothes,
and the child next to him protests :
“Mummy! Tommy’s skinnin’ me alive wif
his club. Make him take it out.”
Tommy: “Shet up, you little ! D’yer
want to be bit with the snake?”
.Tacky shuts up.
“If yer bit,” says Tommy, after a pause,
“ you’ll swell up, an’ smell, an’ turn red an’ green
an’ blue all over till you bust. Won’t he,
mother 5”
“Now then, don’t frighten the child. Go to
sleep,” she says.
The two younger children go to sleep, and now
mid then .Tacky complains of being “skeezed.”
More room is made for him. Presently Tommy
says: “Mother! listen to them (adjective) little
possums. I’d like to screw their blanky necks.”
And Jacky protests drowsily,
“ But they don’t hurt us, the little blanks !”
Mother : “There, I told you you’d teach Jacky
to swear.” But Jacky’s remark makes her smile,
Jacky goes to sleep.
Presently, Tommy asks :
“Mother! Do you think they’ll ever extricate
the (adjective) kangaroo?”
“Lord! How am I to know, child? Goto
sleep.”
“ Will you wake me if the snake comes out?”
“Yes. Go to sleep.”
* # *
Near midnight. The children are all
asleep and she still sits there, sewing and read-
ing by turns. From time to time she glances
round the floor and wall-plate, and, whenever she
hears a noise, she reaches for the stick. The
thunder-storm comes on, and the wind rushing thro’
the cracks in the slab wall threatens to blow out
her candle. She places it on a sheltered part of
the dresser, and fixes up a newspaper to protect
it. At every flash of lightning, the cracks between
the slabs gleam like polished silver. The thunder
rolls, and the rain comes down in torrents.
“ Alligator ” (the dog) lies at full length on the
floor, with his eyes turned towards the partition.
She knows by this that the snake is still there.
There are large cracks in that wall opening under
the floor of the dwelling-house.
She is not a coward, but recent events have
shaken her nerves. A little son of her brother-
in-law was lately bitten by a snake, and died.
Besides, she has not heard from her husband for
six months, and is anxious about him.
He was a drover and started squatting here
when they were married. The drought of 18—
ruined him. He had to sacrifice the I’emnant of
his flock and go droving again. He intends to
move his family into the nearest town when he
comes back, and in the meantime his brother, who
lives on the main road, comes over about once a
month with provisions. The wife has still a
couple of cows, one horse, and a few sheep. The
brother-in-law kills one of the latter occasionally,
gives her what she needs of it, and takes the rest
in return for other provisions.
She is used to being left alone. She once lived
like this for 18 months. As a girl she built, we
suppose, the usual air-castles, but all her girlish
hopes and aspirations are dead. She finds all the
excitement and recreation she needs in the
Young Ladies’ Journal, and, Heaven help her,
takes a pleasure in the fashion-plates.
Her husband is a native, and so is she. He is
careless, but a good husband enough. If he had
the means he would take her to the city and keep
her there like a princess. They are used to being
apart, or at least she is. “No use frettin’, ’ she
says. He may forget sometimes that he is
married, but if he has a good cheque when he
comes back he will give most of it to her. When
he had money he took her to the city several
times—hired a railway sleeping compartment, and
put up at the best hotels. He also bought her a
buggy, but they had to sacrifice it.
The last two children were born in the bush—
one while her husband was bringing a drunken
doctor, by force, to attend on her. She was alone
on this occasion, and very weak. She had been
ill with a fever. She prayed to God to send her
assistance. God sent “ Black Mary,” the
“ whitest ” gin in all the land.
One of her children died while she was here
alone. She rode nineteen miles for assistance,
carrying the dead child.
• * *
It must be near one or two o’clock. The
fire is burning low. Alligator lies with his head
resting on his paws, and watches the wall. He is
not a very beautiful dog to look at, and the light
shows numerous old wounds where the hair will
not grow. He is afraid of nothing on the face of
the earth or under it. He will tackle a bullock as
readily as he will tackle a flea. Ho hates all other
dogs—except kangaroo-dogs—and has a marked
dislike to friends or relations of the family. Tliev
seldom call, however. He sometimes makes
friends with strangers. lie hates snakes, and lias
killed many, but lie will be bitten some day and
die ; most snake-dogs end that way.
Now and then the bushwoman lays down her
work and watches, and listens, and thinks. She
thinks of things in her own life, for there is little
else to think about.
The rain will make the grass grow, and this re-
minds her how she fought a bush-fire once while her
husband was away. The grass was long, and very
dry, and the fire threatened to burn her out. She
put on an old pair of her husband’s trousers, anti
beat out the flames with a green bough till great
drops of sooty perspiration stood out on her fore-
head and ran in streaks down her blackened arms.
The sight of his mother in trousers greatly amused
Tommy, who worked like a little hero by her
side; but the baby howled lustily until his mother
appeared in skirts again. The fire almost
mastered her, but four excited bushmen arrived
just in time.
She thinks how she fought a flood during her
husband’s absence. She stood for hours in the
drenching downpour, and dug a drain to save the
dam across the creek. But she could not save it.
There are things that a bushwoman cannot do.
Next morning the dam was broken, and her heart
was nearly broken too, for she thought how her
husband would feel when he came home and saw
the result of months of labour swept away. She
“cried” then.
She also fought the pleuro-pneumonia, dosed
and bled the few remaining cattle, and wept again
when her two best cows died.
Again, she fought a mad bullock that beseiged
the house for a day. She made bullets and fired
at him thro’ cracks in the slabs, with an old shot-
gun, He was dead in the morning. She skinned
him, and afterwards got 7s. Gel. for the hide.
She also fights the crows and eagles that have
designs on her chickens. Her plan of campaign
is very original. The children cry “Crows,
mother !” and she rushes out and aims a broom-
stick at the birds as though it were a gun, and says
“Bung!” The crows leave in a hurry; they are
cunning, but a woman’s cunning is greater.
Occasionally a bushman in the “ horrors,” or a
villainous-looking “sundowner,” comes and scares
the life out of her. She generally tells the suspi-
cious-looking stranger that her “ husband and two
sons are at work below the dam,” for he always
cunningly enquiries for “the boss.”
Only last week a gallows-faced swagman—
having satisfied himself or been informed that
there were no men on the place—threw his swag
down on the verandah, and demanded tucker.
She gave him something to eat, and then he
expressed his intention of staying for tie night.
It was sundown then. She got a batten from the
sofa, loosened the dog, and confronted the strangi r
—holding the batten in one hand and the dog’s
collar with the other. “ Now you go !” she said.
He looked at her and at the dog, said “ Allright,
mum,” in a cringing tone, and left. She was a
determined-looking woman, and Alligator's yellow

eyes glared unpleasantly. Besides, the dog’s
chawing-up apparatus greatly resembled that of
his namesake.
• * #
She has few pleasures to think of as she
sits here alone by the tire and watches for a snake.
All days are much the same to her; but on Sun-
day afternoon she dresses herself, tidies the
children, smartens-up baby, and goes for a lonely
walk along the bush-track, pushing an old peram-
bulator in front of her. She does this every
Sunday. She takes as much care to make herself
and the children “look smart” as she would if
she were going to “do the block” in Sydney.
There is nothing to see, however, and not a soul
to meet. You might walk for 20 miles along this
track without being able to fix a point in your
mind, unless you are a bushman. It is because of
the madding, everlasting sameness jf the stunted
trees, the quality that makes a new-chum long to
break away and travel as far as trains can go, and
sail as far as ships can sail—and further.
But this bushwoman is used to the loneliness of
it. As a girl-wife she hated it, but now she
would feel strange away from it.
She is glad when her husband returns, but she
does not gush or make a fuss about it. She gets
him something good to eat and tidies up the
children.
She seems contented with her lot. She loves
her children, but has no time to show it. She
seems harsh to them. Her surroundings are not
favourable to the development of the womanly or
sentimental side of nature,
# # *
It must be near morning now, but the 1
clock is in the other room. Her candle is nearly
done ; she forgot that she is out of candles. Some,
more wood must be got to keep the fire up, and stq
she shuts the dog inside and hurries round to the
woodheap. The X’ain lias cleared off. She seizes
a stick, pulls it out, and—crash ! the whole pile
collapses, and nearly frightens her to death.
Yesterday she bargained with a stray black-
fellow to bring her some wood, and while he was
at work she went in search of a missing cow. She-
was absent an hour or so, and the black made>
good use of his time. On her return she was
astonished by seeing a great heap of wood by the
chimney. She gave the black an extra fig of
tobacco, and praised him for not being lazy. He
thanked her, and left with head erect. But he
built the viood-heap hollow.
She is hurt now, and tears spring to her eyes as
she sits down again by the table. She snatches up
a handkerchief to wipe the tears away, but pokes;
her eyes with her bare fingers instead. The hand-
kerchief is full of holes, and site finds that she has
put her thumb through one, and her forefinger
through another.
This makes her laugh suddenly, to the surprise
of the dog. She has a keen, very keen sense of
the ridiculous ; and sometime or another she will
amuse bushmen by relating this incident.
She was amused once before in a manner similar
in some respects. One day she sat down “ to
have a good cry,” as she said - and the old cab
rubbed against her dress and “cried, too.” Then
she “ had to laugh.”
# * *
It must be near daylight now. The room
is very close and hot because of the fire. Alligator
still watches the wall from time to time. Suddenly
he becomes greatly interested ; he draws himself
a few inches nearer the partition, and a thrill
runs through his body. The hair on the back of
his neck begins to bristle, and the battle light is
in his yellow eyes. She knows what this means,
and lays her hand on the stick. The lower
end of one of the partition slabs has a large
crack on each side of it. An evil pair of small,
bright, bead-like eyes glisten at one of these
holes. The snake—a black one—comes slowly out,
about a foot, and moves its head up and down.
The dog lies still, and the woman sits as one fas-
cinated. The snake comes out a foot further. She
lifts her stick, and the reptile, as though suddenly
aware of danger, sticks his head in through the
crack on the other side of the slab, and hurries to
get his tail round after him. Alligator springs,
and his jaws come together with a snap. He
misses this time, for his nose is large, and the
snake’s body close down in the angle formed by
the slabs and the floor. He snaps again as the
tail comes round. He has the snake now, and
tugs it out eighteen inches. Thud, thud, comes the
woman’s club on the ground. Alligator pulls again.
Thud, thud. Alligator pulls some more. Hehasthe
snake out now—a black brute, five feet long. The
head rises to dart about, but the dog has the
enemy close to the neck. He is a big, heavy dog,
but as quick as a terrier. He shakes the snake as
tho’ he felt the curse of Toil in common with
mankind. The eldest boy wakes up, seizes his
stick, and makes to get out of bed, but his mother
forces him back witn a grip of iron. Thud, thud ;
the snake’s back is broken in several places.
Thud, thud ; the head is crushed, and Alligator’s
nose skinned again.
She lifts the mangled reptile on the point of her
stick, carries it to the fire, and throws it in. Then
she piles on the wood and watches the snake burn.
The boy and dog watch, too. She lays her hand,
on the dog’s head, and all the fierce, angry light'
dies out of his yellow eyes. The younger children
,pire quieted, and presently go to sleep. The
dirty-legged boy stands for a moment in his shirt, ,
watching the fire. Presently he looks at her. He
sees the tears in her eyes, and, suddenly throwing t
ins arms round her neck, exclaims :
“Mother, I won’t never go drovin’; blast me,
if I do !”
And she hugs him to her worn-out breast and
kisses him, and they sit thus together while the
sickly daylight breaks over the bush.
HENRY LAWSON.
Impossibles.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I HAVE in my fowl-yard a game-cock of extreme
aristocracy, and a hen of very doubtful parentage.
They have no affinity but their difference of sex
and yet they are inseparable. I never look at
them but I think of Harry Westlake—the bright-
faced, whole-1 rear ted heir of Downton Castle,
svho, kicked out from home for general cussedness,
found himself, at the age of twenty-two, in a
selector’s hut, in Gippsland, with Jenny Mogg,
the selector’s daughter, as his only companion.
Jenny was the hen. Harry’s father, having made
neither his will nor his peace with God, died
suddenly in the hunting-field, and the news that
he was a millionaire and the patron of two
■dozen livings, and hereditary Lord-Lieutenant of
his county, came to Harry as he and the girl sat
■on a log together at the back of the hut, and she
had his coat on.
“It’s a bit rough on the old man, Jenny,” he
-said, when he had lead the letter twice aloud to
her, “ that this news should come before the
fencing’s finished. I reckon the boss won’t like
building the shed without me. And what about
the milk? Who’ll carry that down for you—eh !
little woman ?” and he looked up to see a great
tear in each eye of the girl, through which she had
focussed, out of her recollections of Family Hekalu
stories she had read, a picture of the boy’s going—
Downton Castle, welcoming tenants, and lie, her
lover, in uniform. Poor little woman! —right away
there in the wilds of Gippsland, unhampered of
sociology, these two humans, mortared together
by a common diet —of porridge, mostly— without a
dream of severance, had each grown to be a part of
the other, in obedience to an everlasting law
that finds its antithesis in the Shiels Divorce Act.
And now, without warning, there comes this
hideous news— that, like the bursting of a torpedo,
blows the one to an eminence he did not want,
and the other to a Hell she did not deserve.
The next morning Jenny saw him off, and a
•commercial traveller looking down from the box-
seat saw nothing in tlie face of the girl but the
■calm carved stoicism of heroes who have won
their pedestal.
Harry’s welcome home was of the old English
■order. There were coaches, outsiders, fireworks
and a big feed for the tenantry; but the boy
was not himself. He went about the estate
and tried to make himself agreeable, but the
heart of him was right away in Gippsland,
and Jenny had it in tow. And she, little
bundle of uncultured impetuosity as she was, had
for four years thrown the pebbles of her protest
against the “ divine order ” until one morning she
carried her weary head to the hill-top to “ think
things out.”
There are times in the pottery o f nations when
young countries are being kiln-dried, and Austra-
lia that morning was simmering and sweating in
the process. From the plains below the rising
mist danced in the great heat, and the earth pal-
pitated beneath the blows of a vertical sun thresh-
■ ing moisture out. Sheep with their tongues
; hanging came about the girl unafraid, and her own
■ heart was ci’acking like the soil around.
But for the Victorian Education Act, which
denies to the State-taught the sweet privilege of
groaning biblically, the girl might have said with
Job : “ My breath is corrupt, my days are extinct,
the graves are ready for me.” As it was, hemmed
in with the limited vocabulary of a Gippsland
dialect, she could only mutter again and again,
“ My word, ain’t I miserable !” Suddenly a hand
■was placed upon her shoulder and she looked up
to see, not her lover, nor the man who had carried
milk home and eaten damper, but the lord of the
manor, the English squire, in knickerbockers;
and he, looking down, saw, not the companion of
■other days that he for four years had decked in
the fancy-dress of his own imagining, but a young
woman with coarse features, large red hands, and
a mouth wide open witli astonishment. Truth is
the essence of things hideous made actual, and
these amateurs in life’s methods, there on the
bill-top that morning, took their first big gulp of
it. The pair of them—he on the ancestral couch,
and beneath eider-down, she from the corn-
sack nailed over a home-made stretcher, had built
of crude wishings a mighty column of things
possible, on a foundation of day-dreams ; and now
the whole structure tumbles about their ears, as
they shake hands and talk—of the weather.
Presently they strolled home, drank “ post and
rails ” and ate Johnny cake, and the boss that
night, in honour of the occasion, opened a bottle
of “colonial beer.” As he blew the froth from his
glass, looking at the soft hands of the young
squire, he said : “ You’d make a poor fist at
fencing now I reckon, young ’un.” And Harry,
half in jest and half in earnest, replied :
“ Lend me an old suit to - morrow and pil
show yon I haven’t lost much of it,” i
when he awoke on the morrow the pattern of th
paper and the rattle of milk-cans awoke the oi l
spasm in him. Behind the door there
suit of corduroys ; in these he decked himself a n ,|
left the room and his squiredom behind him witl
his Norfolk suit. And Jenny, who, for fear . J
waking the stranger, had walked so gingerly" i lle *
him in the passage and saw that Time had
“harked back” and the heart of her gave\
mighty “ view halloa” as she saw they were on th'a
old scent again.
That morning’s fencing was an idyll punctuated
with laughter and hyphened with kisses. They
paired in the morticing of a post to talk of
days, and the little woman was never sad but
when ho spoke of England, the women there and
hateful incidents of their separation. Thou
Harry would kiss her cracked lips, and fondle her
plenteous hands, and promise that she too should
s ee and share it all if only she would.
And so (to adopt a convenient and helpm]
phraseology) it came to pass that Harry took his
sweetheart to London and left her in lodgings
while he ran down to the country to tell the
neighbours of the pearl of great price that he had
recovered and make due preparations for the
wedding.
A week before the day appointed a great longhm
took hold of the girl to see her future home, amf
in the disguise of her old Gippsland dress, she
started late in the afternoon for Downton Castle.
And that night, while Harry in the smoking-rooni
of his ancient home raved to his cousin of his girl
and her sweet Australian innocence, she, the girl
herself, was peering from the covert of the woods
at the noble entrance—the iron-spiked gate half-
lowered and the lily pond on the lawn. And then
a cold sense of loneliness rose about her like a
clammy fog, and a dread of days to come—women
are all impressionists—and she, Whistler-like, had
in one moment dashed across the clean canvas of
her prophetic vision a picture of the future-
discordant, incongruous, hideous, and dimmed it
with her tears.
In the morning the old habit, still in full
possession, drove Harry out before the servants
were awake; and he strolled towards a swamp
where moorhens lived. “We, Jenny and I, will
fence this in,” he told himself; “if we get miser-
able —and build a hut in the covert there—and
make a little Gippsland of it, where we can eat
damper, we will.” And now he was by the
water’s side, and there, among the broad, flat
leaves of the lilies, lay Jenny herself. Her up-
turned face had lost its coarseness; her hands
toned down by cold water and the refining influ-
ence of death, were almost lady-like ; and a dab-
chick, disturbed by his approach, dived rather
than witness the boy’s sorrow.
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.

Tom's Old Wife.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There was a dead sikncj at the Orchard Farm
when Tom Morison announced to his mother and
■'sisters that he was going to marry Christina
Cram—she was generally called Kristy Cram.
There was nothing against the woman but her age.
She was good enough looking (for her age);
she was a splendid housewife, having done for
her brother Geordie, for 20 years. But Tom was
25 and Kristy just 22 years older ! Mrs. Mori-
son and she had attended the same day-school
tuid afterwards the same finishing classes in
Edinburgh. A man may not marry his mother;
I believe the law also extends to the grandparents (
but there is no law, canonical or civil, against
Joining your fortunes to your mother’s friend and
von temporary.
Miss Cram had nursed Johnny and had given
him “sweeties,” She had often stayed at The
Orchard in times of sickness or gaiety. The
children looked upon her asan aunt. But latterly,
she stayed at her own place, The Whins. Tom
often went over to see her, when he could get
away fr&fn the farm, and met girls there invited
to be thrown in his way. None of them for him,
however. Gradually Christina saw a change in
Tom’s behaviour and wondered what was wrong.
>V hen she heard his tale she cried with laughter,
but promised not to tell, and for a time Tom
visited her no more. And now a wonder came to
light. In his absence she fell in love ivith Tom .
Von laugh. You sneer. You say, absurd I But
not always the absurd that occurs in life ?
m hen they next met it was at Tullibody Fair.
Tom on his big bay half bred, Kristy in the
Whins’ gig driven by sober Sandy Scobie. And
While Tom stammered “good-day” Kristy
blushed like a girl. Think of the ages ! Tom 2',
Kristy 47. I have seen heaps of love at 47, but up
to Christina’s date always on the male side.
Absurd or not they were married ; Tom let The
Orchard to his mother, Kristy did the same with
The Whins, Geordie from the Big Stone, her own
brother, becoming the tenant. Then they came to
Australia, Many mistakes were made by waiters,
stewards, fellow-passengers and others. But Tom
never laughed or got angry when his wife was
vailed his sister, aunt or mother. The quiet
dignity of Mrs, Morison, stopped the babblers
who began to praise her handsome son. At
Melbourne trouble came. They stopped at a
private place whilst Tom looked for a farm to buy
or rent. Why, oh ! why, do people not mind
their own business ?
In the office of an agent, a snob who had come out
with them was relating with glee how a young man
married to a hag of GO had been in the saloon with
him. Evidently married for her money; old
enough to be his grandmother; thrown away for
life ; and his name is Morison. But that was the
last word the man ever spoke. A lucky or un-
lucky blow behind his ear had stopped his gossip,
muffled his clapper and ended his chatter for over.
The great criminal lawyer could not talk Tom
off. The evidence of the blow was clear, though
the provocation was immense. The jury dis-
agreed. At the next assize a second jury failed to
agree, but at the third time of asking the verdict
was “ guilty of manslaughter with a strong re-
commendation to mercy.”
Oh! wise judge! Oh just judge! Merciful
chances and happy fortune ! A man on the Bench
to save a man ! The sentence was so light as to
be nominal, but the shock killed Mrs. Morison,
She is buried with the other Morisons in Tulli-
body churchyard, a resting-place where curious
effitaphs and stones delight the antiquary. In
the old church, disused since the hideous barn was
built close by for a newer service, in that old
building is a tablet with a strange legend :
“C.M.” and beneath these initials “I loved my
old wife,”
SGOTTY THE WRINKLER.
The Lay of the Swagman.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When the curlew’s lone whistle was heard in the. land,
And wild-duck were seen o’er the river’s white sand ;
When the at night came down from the height
And harassed the sheepfold with ravenous bite ;
When runs were unfenced and shearing commenced,
When labour was scarce and well-recompensed,
Then I ween the next trump at the new woolshed camp
Was not reckoned a scamp, a sundowning scamp—
Never so, years ago.
When railways and rabbits were unknown out here,
A nd grass was as long as carriage was dear,
The teamster’s long waggon, eight horses to pull,
Came up with the rations, went down with the wool ;
Earned more than a wage at the end of each stage,
And put by a trifle to help in old age.
Now, his share of the oyster that grows on the shore v
Is the covering shell, just the shell, nothing more—
Never so, years ago.
When Unions were weak as the light before morn,
And shearers were paid what they asked for the shorn,
The man with the swag was considered a wag
If he asked at the pub., “ Can you put up my hag ?’’
When he comes now to town, no cheque to knock down,
They judge by his togs he’s not worth a crown ;
He stands in the damp outside by the lamp,
And is reckoned a tramp, a sundowning scamp—
Never so, 3 ears ago.
TVCHO.
Dear Bulletin, — I differ greatly from your
judgment of George C. Levey’s “Australian
Cyclopaedia.” It is, I admit, “ a compact handy-
book,” but when that is said all is said. Asa
work of reference it is worthless—even the London
Lit uauy Journal, which is so remarkable an
anti o .ty on Australian matters that it recently
desc 'ioed a wallaby as “a female kangaroo,” can
poin; out defects in it. The first condition re-
quired from a compiler of a book of reference is
that he should be able to discriminate between so-
calle r “ Authorities,” and as Levey, for the major
portion of his book, has depended upon Henniker
Heaton’s “Dictionary of Dates,” it is not sur-
prising his pages bristle with blunders. Even
where Levey has gone for his facts to those really
splendid compilations, Greville’s “Year Book”
and the “ Australian Hand Book ” of Gordon and
Gotch (I have never written a line for either book,
so cannot be suspected of giving any of mv own
work the puff oblique), the transferred information
is mutilated by gross errors of the press. I have
made of the “ Cyclopaedia ” a two hours’ examina-
tion, and in that time marked between three and
four hundred inaccuracies of date and fact, while
as to the selection of matter, the less said the
better. No portion of the contents shows original
research on Levey’s part. The volume, in short,-
is a rechauffe of some good material spoilt in the
re-dishing, the mess being further tainted by
copied inaccuracies. The compiler’s claim that
the work is to date is wholly indefensible as
to much of the information, and both Hayter and
Coghlan would, I am convinced, repudiate any
responsibility for the statistics as they now appear.
Here are a score of blunders taken at random,
many in the first thirty pages “ Bichend,”
given as a Tasmanian town, you will find on no
map. It should be “Bicheno;” so “ Balmorali ”
should be “Balmoral;” “Bowden,” given as the
founder of the V.D.L. settlement, should be
“Bowen.” Assume these are typographical mis-
takes, what of such carelessness of treatment as
marks the paragraphs on “ Banks (Sir Joseph),”
“Sir ‘Jordan’ for ‘Gordon’ Bremer?” Colonel
Barney is mentioned for a failure at settlement,
for lle Was not responsible, and all his really
great engineering works are not alluded to. The
ope fact which justifies Saxe-Bannister’s name
being included in a work of this miscellaneous
character —his opposition to the Torture In-
demnity Bill—is omitted. “ The reforms in con-
vict administration,” consequent oil Bigge’s re*
ports, which Levey specially honours by mention-
■ ing—what were they ? No one can tell. The idea
that Barrington wrote the prologue which includes
the famous lines, “True patriots,” &c., has long
since been abandoned. “George Bruce” is re-
ferred to, but the remarkable romantic episode
associated with his name is not related —because
Heaton didn’t know it. The “article” on the
“Boomerang” would disgrace a State school
infant whose reading books give a later scientific
explanation of the weapon than Levey supplies.
Under “Boat” you learn that “the first boat
built in N.S.W. was The Rosehill Packet, in
1818(1). Turn to page 337, and yon will get a
reference to The Rosehill Packet under “Ship,”
with the date of 1789. Both items are cribbed as
they stand from Heaton, and both as they stand
in Heaton, are inaccurate. Levey, however, is
above being consistent with himself —compare, for
instance, the various historical references to wool
and sheep, and reconcile them if you can. If the
Age editorial staff is mentioned (in order to give
G. C. Levey’s name prominence) why should not
the Argus men have similar treatment? The
allusions to Federation are ludicrously incorrect,
and it is a lucky thing for Levey’s publishers that
D’Ewes (mis-spelt in the book), the magistrate
concerned in the Eureka business, is dead. And
as to the missions—but, hang it all! I can’t take
up your whole space ! Only let me ask why, above
all men, Wentworth should be left out? The
“Cyclo.” is an instance of how a capital idea may
be spoilt in execution--Levey should confine him-
self to editing a dictionary of'the swear-words he
uses.-
PRICE WARUNG.
i Warner Francis .Tulin Plantagenet Hast-
ings, who has just done our Sir Salmon Wi] SO n
the favour of becoming his son-in-law, is thnf
luckless wight, a landless lord. lie is the lineal
descendant of the gentleman whom Shakespeare
has immortalised as the last protector of
Shore, upon whom King Richard did summary
execution : y
Off with his head. Now, by St: Paul, 1 swear
I will not dine until I see it done.
But a newer lustre gilds the family laurels. When
the tenth Earl died in 1789, without issue, the
estates went to the Countess of Moira, and the
Earldom was believed to be dormant. It turned
out, however, that a disreputable, drunken
scandalous-old country clergyman, the Reverend
Thcophilus Henry Hastings, from whose idiosyn-
cracies Thackeray drew the character of the elder
Sir Pitt Crawley, was a lineal descendant of the
second Earl of Huntingdon who died in 15go
nearly 250 years before. Greatly to the horror of
this worthy parson’s relations he declined either
to claim the title himself or to die and give them a
chance. He had made just such a connection as
Sir Pitt Crawley, in his latest days, did with
Betsy Horrock, but lie had married the dotih ful
lady. To the entreaties of his kinsfolk he in-
variably answered: “Wot, make ould Betty
a Countess? Maw. Not I.'’ When Betty
herself urged him to “ stick ' .flop fer his
roights,” he always said, “ Naw, I’ve made thee
an honest ’oonian, but I’m damned if I make thee
a peeress.” This dreadful old man lived on for
nearly thirty years, barring the way all the tiuw
and it was not until 1819 that his nephew suc-
ceeded in establishing his claim and taking (fig
seat as eleventh earl. But from that day to tins
the title has been barren, and nothing was more
common in the early sixties than to see the
twelfth earl sponging on the Dublin stationers
for “two large cards, because Lord Hastings
(his son, the late earl) was going to the levee.”
to which it was that young nobleman’s habit to
proceed upon an jaunting car, fare sixpence. On
the whole, the Knight of Ercildouno is to pay
dearly for his connection with the peerage. His
son gains nothing in particular by his marriage
with the sixth and youngest daughter of the late
Duke of Marlborough, and his daughter will cer-
tainly have the honour of re-gilding the Hunting-
don coronet, while he himself has just been
informed that the Conservatives‘will not make
any gentleman a peer until he has spent .£40,000
in furthering the interests of the party. Up to
the present time lus expenditure in this direction
is computed at £23.000, so the Victorian sheep-
breeder with the flame-coloured whiskers has
£l7,ooomore to spend ere his claim can be enter-
tained. If be spends it on this coming election
and Gladstone beats his friends, he will be further
off than ever when all is done.
